What Is Kalam?
beginner
4 min
The Meaning and Virtue of the Basmalah
Al-Laqqani explains the meaning and virtue of the basmalah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic meaning of the basmalah, the hadith emphasizing its importance, and the definitions of the three divine names: Allah, al-Rahman, and al-Rahim.

Core Idea
The basmalah brings blessing and completeness to every important action.

Al-Laqqani begins his commentary by invoking the basmalah, explaining its syntax and quoting a prophetic hadith that underscores its necessity. This short passage establishes the significance of beginning every important act with the name of Allah. It also provides precise definitions for the divine names, setting the theological groundwork for the rest of the poem.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage from Al-Laqqani's commentary and its English translation. Notice how he starts by saying 'I say seeking help from Allah.'
2
Understand the syntax of bismillah
The passage explains that 'bismillah' is attached to an implied verb. This is a common Arabic grammatical construction.
The implied phrase is 'bi-smi llāhi abtadi’' (with the name of Allah I begin).
3
Learn the hadith and its explanation
The Prophet (peace be upon him) said that any important matter not begun with basmalah is 'aqta' or ajaz' — deficient in blessing. This shows the importance of starting with Allah's name.
The word 'aqta' means deficient, not completely cut off.
4
Distinguish the three names
Allah refers to the necessarily existent essence; al-Rahman is the bestower of abundant blessings; al-Rahim is the bestower of subtle blessings. These are not mere synonyms.
Al-Rahman emphasizes vast mercy, al-Rahim emphasizes specific mercy.
5
Note the transition
Al-Laqqani ends by indicating that the next line begins with 'al-hamdu lillah'. This shows the structure of the poem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله تعالى: أقول مستعينا بالله: بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم. أي أبتدئ بسم الله. وقيل: ابتدائي كائن بسم الله. وبسم الله متعلق بمحذوف تقديره بسم الله أبتدئ. قال عليه الصلاة والسلام: «كل أمر ذي بال لا يبدأ فيه بسم الله فهو أقطع أو أجز». قالوا: أي ناقص قليل البركة. والله علم على الذات الواجب الوجود. والرحمن: المنعم بجلائل النعم. والرحيم: المنعم بدقائقها. وأشار بقوله: الحمد لله.
He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: I say seeking help from Allah: In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. That is, I begin with the name of Allah. And it is said: My beginning is with the name of Allah. And 'in the name of Allah' is attached to an omitted element whose implied meaning is 'with the name of Allah I begin'. He, peace and blessings be upon him, said: 'Every matter of importance that does not begin with the name of Allah is cut off or blemished.' They said: i.e., deficient, little in blessing. 'Allah' is a term for the essence that necessarily exists. 'Al-Rahman': the One who bestows abundant blessings. 'Al-Rahim': the One who bestows delicate blessings. And he indicated by his saying: 'Praise be to Allah.'
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the commentary on the poem's first line. Al-Laqqani first states that he is seeking Allah's help. Then he explains the basmalah syntactically: 'bismillah' is an adverbial phrase connected to an implied verb, meaning 'I begin'. He cites a hadith emphasizing the importance of starting with the basmalah, explaining that 'aqta'' means deficient in blessing. He then defines the three divine names: 'Allah' as the necessarily existent essence, 'al-Rahman' as the bestower of great blessings, and 'al-Rahim' as the bestower of subtle blessings. Finally, he transitions to the next line of the poem by saying 'And he indicated by his saying: al-hamdu lillah'.

Key Terms
بسم الله
الرحمن
الرحيم
أقطع
أجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some readers might think that the basmalah is part of the original poem.
Correction: Al-Laqqani explains the basmalah as part of his commentary, not as a verse of the poem.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of 'أقطع' (aqta') in the hadith cited by al-Laqqani?
A) Completely cut off from reward
B) Deficient and lacking blessing
C) Unacceptable in worship
D) Sinful

Key Takeaway
The basmalah is an essential beginning for every important act, and it introduces the key divine names that describe Allah's attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you start your day, do you consciously say 'Bismillah' before each important task, or do you rush in with your own strength, forgetting that all blessing comes from Him? What have you lost today because you didn't begin with His name?
?
Reflect on the distinction between Allah's abundant blessings (like health and wealth) and His delicate blessings (like the ability to taste, to think, to feel). Which do you take for granted more? How can you increase your gratitude for the delicate ones?
?
The hadith says every important matter without basmalah is deficient. Think of a recent action you considered successful but did not start with basmalah. In what ways was it truly deficient in blessing? Will you commit to starting your most important tasks tomorrow with His name?

What Is Kalam?

4 min
Two Reasons for Beginning with Allah's Name and Three Opinions on Basmala
The commentator lists the two reasons for starting with basmala and reports the three famous opinions on its Quranic status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two reasons for beginning with the name of Allah and the three famous opinions on the basmala's status in the Quran.

Core Idea
The basmala's placement reflects prophetic teaching, not just revelation order.

The commentator begins by explaining why we start with Allah's name: following the Quran's arrangement and following the prophetic command. He then clarifies that the Quran's order is tawqifi, not based on revelation sequence. This leads to the question of whether the basmala is part of every sura, and he presents the three major scholarly opinions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before interpreting.
Notice the discussion on the ba' and the subsequent reasons.
2
Identify the two reasons
The passage presents two reasons for beginning with Allah's name: following the Qur'an and following the prophetic command. Find each one.
3
Understand the tawqifi arrangement
The commentator explains that the Quran's arrangement is divinely instructed, not based on revelation order. This is key to understanding the basmala's position.
This connects to the learning objective.
4
Compare the three opinions
The passage lists the Maliki, Shafi'i, and Hanafi views on the basmala's status. Note the differences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في البسملة والحمدلة وما يتعلق بهما
الله الرّحمن
بالكتاب العزيز (۱) ، ولقوله : كلُّ أمرٍ ذِي بَالٍ لا يُبدأ فيه ببسم الله الرَّ.
ما قيل: إن سوء الأدب مع هذا المعنى المقصود بالاستعانة لا يزال موجودا. وأمَّا مُتعلّق الباء ، فقد اختلفوا فيه أيضاً، هل يُقدَّر فعلا أو اسمًا ؟ وعلى كل واحد منهما هل يقدر متقدماً أو متأخرًا ؟ وعلى كلّ من هذه الأوجه هل يقدَّر عاماً كأبتدئ وابتدائي، أو يقدَّرُ من جنس المشروع فيه فيقال: أؤلف أو تأليفي، إذا كان المشروع فيه تأليفا، وأشرب أو شُرْبي، إذا كنت شارِعاً في الشرب؟ وهلم جرا ، والأصحُ : أَنَّه يقدر فعلا ؛ لأنَّ الأصل في العمل للأفعال ، ويقدَّر متأخّرًا ، لكي لا يتقدم على اسم الله تعالى شيء لا في اللفظ ولا في التقدير ، ومن جنس المشروع فيه ، لأنَّ كلَّ شارع في شيء يجعل التسمية مبدأ لذلك الفعل.
. .
6
وفي هذا القدر كفاية . محي. (۱) ذكرَ الشَّارِحُ سَببَيْن للابتداء باسم الله تعالى، الأول: الاقتداء بالكتاب العزيز، والمراد به القرآن الكريم ، وليس معنى ذلك أن أول شيءٍ بُدئ بنزوله من القرآن الكريم هو البسملة ؛ فإنَّ المعروفَ أَنَّ أَوَّل شيءٍ نزل به جبريل من القرآن الكريم هو قوله تعالى: ﴿اقْرَأْ بِاسْمِ رَبِّكَ الَّذِي خَلَقَ﴾ الآيات مِن أوّل سورة العلق، وإنَّما المقصود أن أوّل القرآن الكريم في ترتيبه الذي أمر به الرسول صلوات الله وسلامه عليه بإرشادِ جبريل هو ذلك ؛ فإنَّ المعلوم أنَّ جبريل كان يقول للنبي عقب نزل شيءٍ مِن القرآن : موضع للنَّبيِّ في مكان كذا ، بين وكذا ، وكان الرسول الله يأمر أصحابه بذلك ، وشيءٌ آخر يدل على ذلك ، وهو: أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم كان ينزل إليه جبريل في رمضانَ مِن كلّ عام فيُدارسه القرآن ، وكان رسولُ الله يدارس زيداً بعد مدارسة جبريل ؛ فثبت بهذا أنَّ القرآن الكريم كان مرتبا ترتيبا توقيفيا غير ترتيب نزوله، وهو على الوجه الذي بلغنا متواترا التواتر الذي يفيدُ القطع، ثمَّ إن ابتداء القرآن بالبسملة لا يستلزم أنَّها جزء منه ، فإن الأكل وغيره من الأفعال تبدأ بالبسملة ، وليست هي جزءاً من شيء منها ، وفي هذه المسألة أقوال كثيرة ولكن أشهرها ثلاثة:
الأوّل — وهو مذهب مالك رحمه الله - أنها بعض آية من سورة النمل ، لا غير . والثاني — وهو مذهب الشافعي رحمه الله - أنها آية من سورة الفاتحة ومن كل سورة من سور القرآن . والثالث — وهو المعروف من مذهب الحنفية — أنها آية مستقلة نزلت ليفصل بها بين السور ، وليست آية الفاتحة بخصوصها، ولا من سورة أخرى، غير النمل فإنها بعض آية من أثنائها .
Discussion on the basmala and the hamdala and what pertains to them. Allah, the Most Gracious. By the Mighty Book, and by his saying: 'Every matter of importance that does not begin with the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful...' It was said: Ill-manneredness with this meaning intended by seeking assistance is still present. As for what the ba' relates to, they also differed: is it estimated as a verb or a noun? In each case, is it estimated as preceding or following? In each of these aspects, is it estimated as general, like 'I begin' or 'my beginning', or is it estimated as being of the same type as the intended action, so one says 'I compose' or 'my composition' if the intended action is composition, and 'I drink' or 'my drinking' if you are about to drink? And so on. The correct view is that it is estimated as a verb, because the basis of action is verbs, and it is estimated as following, so that nothing precedes the name of Allah, Most High, neither in wording nor in estimation, and of the same type as the intended action, because everyone who begins an action makes the naming the commencement of that action. And in this amount is sufficiency. (1) The commentator mentioned two reasons for beginning with the name of Allah, Most High. The first: following the Mighty Book, by which is meant the Noble Qur'an. This does not mean that the first thing revealed of the Noble Qur'an was the basmala; for it is well known that the first thing Jibril brought down of the Noble Qur'an is the saying of the Most High: 'Read in the name of your Lord who created'—verses from the beginning of Surat al-'Alaq. Rather, the intended meaning is that the first of the Noble Qur'an in its arrangement, which the Messenger (peace and blessings upon him) ordered by the guidance of Jibril, is that. For it is known that Jibril used to say to the Prophet after the revelation of something from the Qur'an: 'Place for the Prophet in such-and-such a place, between such-and-such.' And the Messenger of Allah would command his Companions with that. Another thing indicates that: the Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings upon him) had Jibril descend to him in Ramadan every year to study the Qur'an with him, and the Messenger of Allah would study with Zayd after the study with Jibril. Thus it is established that the Noble Qur'an was arranged with a tawqifi arrangement different from its order of revelation, and it is on the basis that reached us as mutawatir, which yields certainty. Then, that the Qur'an begins with the basmala does not entail that it is part of it, for eating and other actions begin with the basmala, yet it is not part of any of them. In this issue there are many sayings, but the most famous of them are three: The first—and it is the school of Malik (may Allah have mercy on him)—that it is part of a verse from Surat al-Naml, and nothing else. The second—and it is the school of al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him)—that it is a verse from Surat al-Fatiha and from every sura of the Qur'an. The third—and it is the well-known view from the Hanafi school—that it is an independent verse revealed to separate between suras, and it is not a verse of Surat al-Fatiha specifically, nor from another sura, except al-Naml, for it is part of a verse from within it.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage introduces the discussion on the basmala and hamdala. It begins by addressing the grammatical and interpretive issues of the ba' in 'bismillah'—whether it attaches to a verb or a noun, and whether it is general or specific to the action at hand. The commentator then turns to the reasons for beginning with Allah's name. The first reason is following the Noble Qur'an, but he clarifies that this does not mean the basmala was the first revealed verse; rather, it is the first in the tawqifi (divinely instructed) arrangement. He argues this from the practice of Jibril and the Prophet in arranging the Qur'an. He then notes that the Qur'an opening with basmala does not prove it is part of the Qur'an itself—just as eating and other acts begin with it without it being part of them. Finally, he reports the three famous opinions: the Maliki view that it is only a part of a verse in Surah al-Naml, the Shafi'i view that it is a verse of al-Fatiha and of every sura, and the Hanafi view that it is an independent verse for separation between suras.

Key Terms
بسملة
حمدلة
ترتيب توقيفي
متواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that because the Qur'an begins with basmala, it is necessarily a verse of every sura.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the basmala's position does not entail it is part of the Qur'an; it is used at the start of many acts without being part of them.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the significance of the Qur'an's arrangement being tawqifi?
A) It means the Qur'an was revealed in its current order from the beginning.
B) It means the order was taught by Jibril and the Prophet, different from revelation order.
C) It means the basmala is part of every sura.
D) It means the Qur'an was arranged by the Companions.

Key Takeaway
The basmala's importance is affirmed, but its exact status as a Quranic verse is debated among scholars, reflecting careful consideration of prophetic guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentator stresses beginning every important matter with Allah's name. When was the last time you started a task — no matter how small — with genuine, heartfelt 'Bismillah'? Will you commit to saying it with presence for every task tomorrow?
?
The passage distinguishes between reciting basmala as a mere formula and truly seeking Allah's assistance. Are your daily 'Bismillahs' a shield of mindfulness or just a habit? What will it take to transform that word into a lifeline?
?
You now know that even the placement of the basmala is a matter of prophetic guidance and scholarly precision. Does your reverence for the Qur'an's arrangement — and for every letter in the basmala — reflect this weight? If not, what first step will you take today to deepen that reverence?

The Divine Names and the Proper Name of Allah
The author defines three divine names and proves that 'Allah' is a personal proper noun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of the divine names Allah, al-Rahman, and al-Rahim, and the argument that the word 'Allah' is a personal proper name rather than a universal term.

Core Idea
The name 'Allah' uniquely identifies God without relying on attributes.

This lesson opens with foundational divine names: Allah, al-Rahman, al-Rahim. While the first two are clear, the author devotes special attention to proving that 'Allah' is a proper name, not a descriptive term. This distinction is crucial for understanding the kalimah of tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Begin by reading the Arabic definitions of Allah, al-Rahman, and al-Rahim. Notice that Allah is defined as a proper noun for the necessarily existent self, while al-Rahman and al-Rahim are descriptive of His bestowal of favors.
These definitions set the stage for the deeper argument about the word 'Allah'.
2
Understand 'personal proper noun'
The author presents the chosen view that 'Allah' is a علم شخصي (personal proper noun). This means it refers directly to a specific individual (the divine essence) and not through universal concepts.
Contrast this with common nouns that indicate a class.
3
Follow the proof from tawhid
The author argues that if 'Allah' were a universal term, the statement 'la ilaha illa Allah' would not single out Allah in divinity. Since Muslims agree that this statement is the word of tawhid, it must indicate uniqueness, so 'Allah' must be a proper name.
This is the core logical argument.
4
Appreciate the consensus
The author uses the consensus of Muslims and even monotheists from all times as a supporting evidence. This shows the argument is not just philosophical but grounded in communal acceptance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في البسملة والحمدلة وما يتعلق بهما
والله : عَلَمٌ على الذات الواجب الوجود
والرَّحمن: المُنْعِم بجلائل النعم
والرَّحيم : المُنْعِم بدقائقها
(١) يشير إلى أنَّ المُختار أنَّ لفظ الجلالة علمٌ شخصي، بمعنى أن مدلوله معين في الخارج، لا أَنَّه قامت به مُشخصات كالبياض والطول؛ لأنَّ ذلك المعنى مستحيل بالنسبة لله. والمقصود من هذا أن لفظ الجلالة ليس دالا على معنى كُليّ؛ لأنه لو كان كذلك لم تكن كلمة التوحيد ـ وهي قولنا لا إله إلا الله دالة على إفراده سبحانه بالألوهية دون سائر من عداه، وقد أجمع المسلمون، بل الموحدون من جميع الملل في جميع الأعصار على أَنَّ هذه الكلمة دالة على التّوحيد، ومن أجل ذلك سُمِّيت كلمة التوحيد، فوجب ألا يكون لفظ الجلالة دالا على معنى كلي، فثبت أنه دال على معنى مُشخّص بالمعنى الذي ذكرناه.
Discourse on the basmalah and the hamdalah and what pertains to them. And Allah: a proper noun for the self that is necessarily existent. And al-Rahman: the bestower of great favors. And al-Rahim: the bestower of subtle favors. (1) He indicates that the chosen view is that the word of majesty is a personal proper noun, meaning that its referent is particular in external existence, not that it is endowed with particularizing attributes like whiteness and tallness; because that meaning is impossible with respect to Allah. And the intent of this is that the word of majesty does not indicate a universal meaning; because if it were so, the word of tawhid — which is our saying 'there is no god but Allah' — would not indicate His uniqueness in divinity to the exclusion of others. And the Muslims, rather the monotheists from all religious communities in all ages, have agreed that this word indicates tawhid, and for that reason it was called the word of tawhid. So it is necessary that the word of majesty not indicate a universal meaning, and thus it is established that it indicates a particular meaning in the sense we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a definition and a proof. It first defines three divine names: Allah is a proper noun for the necessarily existent self; al-Rahman is the bestower of great favors; al-Rahim is the bestower of subtle favors. Then, in a footnote, the author argues that the word 'Allah' is a personal proper noun (علم شخصي), meaning it refers to a specific individual (the divine essence) and not a universal concept. He proves this by noting that if 'Allah' were a universal term, the statement 'la ilaha illa Allah' would not serve to single out Allah in divinity. Since there is consensus that this statement is the word of tawhid, it must indicate exclusivity, and thus the word 'Allah' must denote a specific referent. The author also clarifies that the particularity is not by accidents (like whiteness or tallness) but by being the particular essence itself.

Key Terms
لفظ الجلالة
علم شخصي
كلمة التوحيد
الذات الواجب الوجود
الرحمن
الرحيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'Allah' is a derived name (e.g., from al-ilah) and implies a universal concept.
Correction: The author asserts that 'Allah' is a personal proper noun, not derived, and directly refers to the divine essence.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the word 'Allah' must be a proper name and not a universal term?
A) Because it is derived from al-ilah.
B) Because the kalimah of tawhid would not indicate uniqueness otherwise.
C) Because it has no meaning.
D) Because Arabs used it for their idols.

Key Takeaway
The name 'Allah' is a personal proper noun that directly refers to the divine essence, not a universal term, and this is proven by the function of the kalimah of tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'La ilaha illa Allah', do you truly believe that you are naming the unique Essence, or do you unconsciously treat it as a description that could apply to any 'god'? How does this understanding change the weight of the statement on your tongue?
?
If the name 'Allah' were just a generic title, your declaration of faith would be empty. Have you ever felt the danger of reciting the shahada without realizing its specific, exclusive reference? What would it mean to know that you are calling upon the very Essence, not just a label?

What Is Kalam?

The Linguistic Definition of al-Hamd
Definition of al-hamd with conditions and distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise linguistic meaning of al-hamd (praise) according to classical kalam scholars, its essential conditions, and how it differs from related terms like madih, shukr, and thana'.

Core Idea
Al-hamd is praise for a beautiful voluntary act, whether or not it follows a favor.

This passage unpacks the word al-hamd, which appears in the opening of the Qur'an. The commentator defines it linguistically and distinguishes it from similar terms to prevent confusion. Understanding this definition is crucial for a deeper grasp of 'al-hamdu lillah' and the foundations of theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of hamd
Locate the commentator's definition: 'vocal eulogy for a beautiful voluntary act, in the manner of exaltation and veneration, whether in return for a favor or not.'
Notice the inclusion of 'whether in return for a favor or not' – this distinguishes hamd from shukr.
2
Identify the three conditions
Break down the definition: (1) vocal eulogy, (2) for a beautiful act, (3) voluntary. Each condition excludes certain types of praise.
The condition 'voluntary' is key – it excludes involuntary attributes like height.
3
Understand the exception about favors
Hamd can be for a favor or not; this separates it from gratitude (shukr), which requires a favor.
4
Distinguish hamd from madih and shukr
Note: madih can be for involuntary attributes, while hamd cannot. Shukr is only in response to a favor.
The four terms (hamd, madih, shukr, thana') are often conflated.
5
Reflect on the two types of hamd
The passage mentions temporal hamd (from creatures) and eternal hamd (from Allah). The definition focuses on the temporal kind, but the eternal is also valid.
This resolves the apparent limitation of 'vocal' to creatures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في البسملة والحمدلة وما يتعلق بهما
(١)
التَّعظيم والتبجيل، سواء كانَ في مُقابَلَةِ نعمةٍ، أم لا (١) .
(١) ههنا أربع ألفاظ تتقارب معانيها ، ولكن بين كل واحد منها والآخر تخالفا ما، فوجب أن تحدد معنى كل واحد منها وننسبه إلى الآخر؛ ليتضح أمرها تمام الاتضاح، أما الألفاظ الأربعة فهي : الحمد ، والمدح ، والشكر والثناء). وأما معانيها:
اللغة فالحمد في هو ما ذكره الشارح بقوله: (الثَّناء باللسان على الفعل الجميل الاختياري، على جهة التعظيم والتبجيل، سواء كان في مقابلة نعمة، أم لا). وقبل أن نشرح لك هذا التعريف نقول لك: إنَّ في قوله (الثَّناء باللسان) تخصيصا للحمد بالحادث منه ، وذلك أنَّ الحمد ضربان: أحدهما: حمد حادث وهو ما وقع من الخلق، سواء أكان المحمود قديما كقولنا : الحمد الله ، أم كان المحمود حادثًا كما تقول: حمدتُ زيدا، وثانيهما: حمد قديم ، وهو ما كان من الله تعالى، سواء أكان المحمود قديما كقوله سبحانه: الحمد الله ، أم كان المحمود حادثا كما في حمده سبحانه لرسله ولعباده الصالحين، وإنما كان في تعريف الشارح تخصيص الحمد بالحادث منه لأنه جعله ثناءً باللسان، واللسان جارحة الكلام للحوادث لا غير ، ويمكن أن يجاب بأحد جوابين ؛ أولهما: أنَّ مرادَهُ تعريف الحمد الحادث ، فـ(أل) في المعرَّف للعهد، وثانيهما: أنَّ مراده تعريف الحمد مطلقا، وقوله (باللسان) لا يراد منه الجارحة المخصوصة ، ولكن المراد منه هنا الكلام مطلقاً ، وبعد : فنقول: إن قوله (على الفعل الجميل) يراد به أن يكون الفعل المحمود عليه جميلاً في اعتقاد المحمود بزعم الحامد، سواء أكان جميلاً في النظر العام الصحيح كفعل الخيرات، أم لم يكن جميلا في هذا النظر كنهب الأعمار والأموال في نحو قول القائل:
نَهَبْتَ مِنَ الأَعْمَارِ مَا لَوْ حَوَيْتَهُ لَهُنَّتِ الدُّنْيَا بِأَنَّكَ خَالِدُ وقوله (الاختياري) قيد لإخراج ما هو جميل لكنه حاصل بغير اختيار المحمود وفعله، كطول قامة زيد، وإشراق لونه ؛ فإنّ الثَّناء عليه بمثل ذلك يسمى مدحا ، ولا يسمى حمدا ، واعلم أنَّ المحمود عليه غير المحمود به في الحقيقة والماهية ، لأن المحمود عليه هو الفعل الصادر من المحمود، والمحمود به هو الكلام الصادر من الحامد، ثم قد يتحد مَاصَدَقُهما ذاتا ، ويختلفان اعتبارًا ، كما إذا أكرمك زيد فقلت : زيد كريم ، فإن الكريم من حيث كونه باعنا لك على الحمد يقال له : محمود عليه، ومن حيث كونه مدلول العبارة يقال له: محمود به، وقد يختلفان ذاتا ،واعتبارا ، كما إذا أكرمك زيد فقلت: زيد عالمٌ ، فإنّ المحمود عليه هو الكرم، والمحمود به هو العلم.
Discourse on the basmalah and the hamdalah and what pertains to them. (1) Exaltation and veneration, whether in return for a favor or not (1). (1) Here are four terms whose meanings are close, but between each one and the other there is some difference, so it is necessary to define the meaning of each one and relate it to the other, so that its matter becomes completely clear. The four terms are: al-hamd (praise), al-madih (commendation), al-shukr (thankfulness), and al-thana' (eulogy). As for their meanings: In language, al-hamd is what the commentator mentioned in his saying: 'vocal eulogy for a beautiful voluntary act, in the manner of exaltation and veneration, whether in return for a favor or not.' Before we explain this definition to you, we say to you: In his saying 'vocal eulogy' there is a specification of praise to the temporal kind, because praise is of two types: one is temporal praise, which is what occurs from creatures, whether the praised one is eternal like our saying 'praise be to Allah' or the praised one is temporal as when you say 'I praised Zayd'; and the second is eternal praise, which is from Allah Almighty, whether the praised one is eternal like His saying 'praise be to Allah' or the praised one is temporal as in His praise of His messengers and righteous servants. The specification of praise to the temporal in the commentator's definition is because he made it vocal eulogy, and the tongue is the organ of speech for temporal beings only. It can be answered with one of two answers: first, that he intended the definition of temporal praise, so the definite article 'al' in the defined term is for the specific known; second, that he intended the definition of praise absolutely, and his saying 'vocal' does not mean the specific organ, but rather here it means speech absolutely. Afterward, we say: His saying 'for a beautiful act' intends that the act praised be beautiful in the belief of the praised one according to the claim of the praiser, whether it is beautiful in sound general opinion like doing good deeds, or not beautiful in this opinion like plundering lives and wealth in the saying of the poet: 'You have plundered from lifetimes what, if you had amassed, the world would be humbled that you are immortal.' And his saying 'voluntary' is a restriction to exclude what is beautiful but occurs without the choice and action of the praised one, like Zayd's tall stature and the radiance of his complexion; eulogy for such things is called commendation (madih) and is not called praise (hamd). Know that the thing praised for and the thing praised with are different in reality and essence, because the thing praised for is the act emanating from the praised one, and the thing praised with is the speech emanating from the praiser. Then they may be the same in referent but differ in consideration, as when Zayd shows you generosity and you say 'Zayd is generous'; thus 'generous' in terms of being a cause for your praise is called 'praised for', and in terms of being the meaning of the expression is called 'praised with'. And they may differ in referent and consideration, as when Zayd shows you generosity and you say 'Zayd is knowledgeable'; then the thing praised for is generosity, and the thing praised with is knowledge.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a detailed linguistic definition and classification. The author first notes that there are four related terms: al-hamd, al-madih, al-shukr, and al-thana'. He then gives the definition of al-hamd from the commentator: vocal eulogy for a beautiful voluntary act, with exaltation and veneration, whether or not in response to a favor. He explains that 'vocal' specifies temporal praise, but this is resolved by noting either that it defines temporal praise specifically or that 'vocal' means speech in general. The definition includes 'beautiful act' meaning beautiful in the praiser's view, even if not objectively good. The 'voluntary' condition excludes praiseworthy but involuntary attributes like height, which are called madih not hamd. Finally, the author distinguishes between the act that prompts praise (muhmud 'alayh) and the speech that expresses the praise (muhmud bihi), giving examples of when they coincide or differ.

Key Terms
الحمد
المدح
الشكر
الثناء
المحمود عليه
المحمود به

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-hamd (praise) and al-shukr (thankfulness) are synonymous.
Correction: Al-hamd can be for any beautiful voluntary act whether or not it is a favor, while al-shukr is specifically in response to a favor.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is praising someone for their tall stature called madih and not hamd?
A) Because tall stature is not a beautiful act.
B) Because the praised person did not voluntarily choose it.
C) Because it is not done with the tongue.
D) Because it is a response to a favor.

Key Takeaway
Al-hamd is vocal eulogy for a beautiful voluntary act with exaltation, whether or not it follows a favor, and differs from madih (for involuntary traits) and shukr (only for favors).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'alhamdulillah' after a meal or a blessing, do you realize that hamd is not only for that favor but for all of Allah's beautiful voluntary acts? Think about one thing Allah has done today that was not a direct favor to you but still deserves praise. Will you praise Him for it today?
?
The lesson distinguishes between the act you praise someone for (mahmud 'alayh) and the words you use (mahmud bihi). Reflect on a time you praised a person but your words did not match the actual deed. How can you ensure your praise is truthful and sincere, both for people and for Allah?
?
Consider an involuntary quality you have that others might admire, like intelligence or beauty. The lesson says praise for such traits is madih, not hamd. Does this humble you to realize that the only true praise (hamd) is for voluntary acts? How does this affect your view of your own accomplishments and your gratitude to Allah for giving you the ability to choose good?

The Four Related Terms and the Linguistic Definition of Al-Hamd
Introduces four related terms and gives the linguistic definition of al-hamd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic definition of al-hamd and how it differs from three related terms.

Core Idea
Al-hamd is vocal praise for a beautiful voluntary act, not necessarily in response to a favor.

Before diving into theological arguments, Sanusi clarifies key terms that often cause confusion. Al-hamd is one of four closely related words, and its precise definition sets the foundation for understanding praise in the Islamic tradition. This passage presents the definition given by the commentator and explains its components.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four words
Read the first few lines to find the four terms: al-hamd, al-madh, al-shukr, al-thana'. Notice they are close but distinct.
This classification helps avoid mixing them up.
2
Read the definition of al-hamd
The commentator defines al-hamd as 'vocal eulogy upon the beautiful voluntary act, in a manner of glorification and veneration, whether in return for a favor or not.' Look for each component.
3
Understand the key components
Focus on 'vocal eulogy', 'beautiful voluntary act', 'glorification and veneration', and 'whether in return for a favor or not'. Each component restricts or expands the meaning.
The last part shows that al-hamd is not limited to thanking for favors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ههنا أربع ألفاظ تتقارب معانيها ، ولكن بين كل واحد منها والآخر تخالفا ما، فوجب أن تحدد معنى كل واحد منها وننسبه إلى الآخر؛ ليتضح أمرها تمام الاتضاح، أما الألفاظ الأربعة فهي : الحمد ، والمدح ، والشكر والثناء). وأما معانيها:

نَهَبْتَ مِنَ الأَعْمَارِ مَا لَوْ حَوَيْتَهُ لَهُنَّتِ الدُّنْيَا بِأَنَّكَ خَالِدُ وقوله (الاختياري) قيد لإخراج ما هو جميل لكنه حاصل بغير اختيار المحمود وفعله، كطول قامة زيد، وإشراق لونه ؛ فإنّ الثَّناء عليه بمثل ذلك يسمى مدحا ، ولا يسمى حمدا ، واعلم أنَّ المحمود عليه غير المحمود به في الحقيقة والماهية ، لأن المحمود عليه هو الفعل الصادر من المحمود، والمحمود به هو الكلام الصادر من الحامد، ثم قد يتحد مَاصَدَقُهما ذاتا ، ويختلفان اعتبارًا ، كما إذا أكرمك زيد فقلت : زيد كريم ، فإن الكريم من حيث كونه باعنا لك على الحمد يقال له : محمود عليه، ومن حيث كونه مدلول العبارة يقال له: محمود به، وقد يختلفان ذاتا ،واعتبارا ، كما إذا أكرمك زيد فقلت: زيد عالمٌ ، فإنّ المحمود عليه هو الكرم، والمحمود به هو العلم.
Here are four words whose meanings are close, but between each one and the other there is some difference, so it is necessary to define the meaning of each one and relate it to the other, so that their matter becomes fully clear. As for the four words, they are: al-hamd (praise), al-madh (commendation), al-shukr (thanksgiving), and al-thana' (eulogy). As for their meanings: In language, al-hamd is what the commentator mentioned in his saying: 'Vocal eulogy upon the beautiful voluntary act, in a manner of glorification and veneration, whether it is in return for a favor or not.' Before we explain this definition to you, we say to you: In his saying 'vocal eulogy' there is specification of al-hamd to the temporal kind, because verily al-hamd is two types: one of them: temporal al-hamd, which is what occurs from creatures, whether the praised one is eternal like our saying 'Praise be to God', or the praised one is temporal like when you say 'I praised Zayd'; and the second one: eternal al-hamd, which is what is from God Almighty, whether the praised one is eternal like His saying 'Praise be to God', or the praised one is temporal as in His praise of His messengers and His righteous servants. The specification of al-hamd in the definition of the commentator to the temporal kind is because he made it vocal eulogy, and the tongue is the organ of speech for temporal beings only. It can be answered with one of two answers: first: that his intent is the definition of temporal al-hamd, so 'al' in the defined is for specific reference; second: that his intent is the definition of al-hamd absolutely, and his saying 'vocal' does not intend the specific organ, but what is intended here is speech absolutely. After that, we say: his saying 'upon the beautiful act' intends that the act upon which praise is given be beautiful in the belief of the praised according to the praiser, whether it is beautiful in sound general consideration like doing good deeds, or not beautiful in this consideration like plundering of ages and wealth in the poet's saying: 'You plundered from the ages what, if you had possessed it, the world would have congratulated itself that you are eternal.' His saying 'voluntary' is a restriction to exclude what is beautiful but occurs without the choice and action of the praised, such as Zayd's tall stature and the brightness of his complexion; for eulogy upon such is called commendation, and is not called praise. And know that the object of praise (al-mahmud 'alayhi) and the means of praise (al-mahmud bihi) differ in reality and essence, because the object of praise is the action emanating from the praised, and the means of praise is the speech emanating from the praiser. Then they may coincide in referent essentially and differ in consideration, as when Zayd honors you and you say 'Zayd is generous', for the generous, in terms of being a cause for your praise, is called object of praise, and in terms of being the meaning of the expression, is called means of praise. And they may differ both essentially and in consideration, as when Zayd honors you and you say 'Zayd is learned', for the object of praise is generosity, and the means of praise is knowledge.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage defines the linguistic term 'al-hamd' (praise) as part of a discussion of four related terms. The author first introduces the four words: al-hamd, al-madh, al-shukr, and al-thana'. He then presents the definition of al-hamd given by the commentator: 'Vocal eulogy upon the beautiful voluntary act, in a manner of glorification and veneration, whether in return for a favor or not.' The author explains that this definition specifies al-hamd as a temporal act because it uses 'vocal' which refers to the tongue, a temporal organ. He notes that al-hamd can be divided into two types: temporal (from creatures) and eternal (from God). He then addresses a potential objection regarding the use of 'vocal' for God's praise by offering two answers: either the definition is meant for temporal praise specifically, or 'vocal' is interpreted broadly as speech. Next, he clarifies 'beautiful act' as any act deemed beautiful by the praiser, even if not objectively sound. 'Voluntary' excludes involuntary beautiful qualities like height, which would be called commendation not praise. Finally, he distinguishes between the object of praise (the act) and the means of praise (the speech), showing how they can coincide or differ in referent.

الشكر
الثناء

Misconception: Many think al-hamd is always in response to a favor, like thanking someone.
Correction: The definition explicitly states it can be without a favor, showing al-hamd is broader than shukr.

According to the definition, which of the following is NOT a component of al-hamd?

The Two Types of Al-Hamd: Temporal and Eternal
Divides al-hamd into temporal and eternal, and answers an objection about 'vocal'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the division of al-hamd into temporal and eternal, and how to reconcile the word 'vocal' in the definition with God's praise.

Core Idea
Al-hamd can be temporal (from creatures) or eternal (from God), even though 'vocal' seems to limit it to temporal beings.

The definition of al-hamd includes the word 'vocal', which might suggest it only applies to temporal beings with tongues. But we also speak of God praising, so how can that be? Sanusi addresses this by dividing al-hamd into two types and offering two possible answers to the objection. This lesson unpacks that discussion.

Guided Path
1
Learn the two types of al-hamd
The author divides al-hamd into temporal (from creatures) and eternal (from God). Both can be for an eternal or temporal object of praise.
Examples are given: 'Praise be to God' is temporal praise of the Eternal; God's praise of prophets is eternal praise of temporal beings.
2
Understand the objection
The objection is: the definition says 'vocal eulogy', and the tongue is a temporal organ, so how can eternal praise be vocal?
3
Review the two answers
First answer: The definition is only for temporal praise, so 'al' in 'al-hamd' refers to that specific type. Second answer: 'Vocal' here means 'speech' in general, not necessarily the physical tongue.
Both answers preserve the possibility of eternal praise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ههنا أربع ألفاظ تتقارب معانيها ، ولكن بين كل واحد منها والآخر تخالفا ما، فوجب أن تحدد معنى كل واحد منها وننسبه إلى الآخر؛ ليتضح أمرها تمام الاتضاح، أما الألفاظ الأربعة فهي : الحمد ، والمدح ، والشكر والثناء). وأما معانيها:
اللغة فالحمد في هو ما ذكره الشارح بقوله: (الثَّناء باللسان على الفعل الجميل الاختياري، على جهة التعظيم والتبجيل، سواء كان في مقابلة نعمة، أم لا). وقبل أن نشرح لك هذا التعريف نقول لك: إنَّ في قوله (الثَّناء باللسان) تخصيصا للحمد بالحادث منه ، وذلك أنَّ الحمد ضربان: أحدهما: حمد حادث وهو ما وقع من الخلق، سواء أكان المحمود قديما كقولنا : الحمد الله ، أم كان المحمود حادثًا كما تقول: حمدتُ زيدا، وثانيهما: حمد قديم ، وهو ما كان من الله تعالى، سواء أكان المحمود قديما كقوله سبحانه: الحمد الله ، أم كان المحمود حادثا كما في حمده سبحانه لرسله ولعباده الصالحين، وإنما كان في تعريف الشارح تخصيص الحمد بالحادث منه لأنه جعله ثناءً باللسان، واللسان جارحة الكلام للحوادث لا غير ، ويمكن أن يجاب بأحد جوابين ؛ أولهما: أنَّ مرادَهُ تعريف الحمد الحادث ، فـ(أل) في المعرَّف للعهد، وثانيهما: أنَّ مراده تعريف الحمد مطلقا، وقوله (باللسان) لا يراد منه الجارحة المخصوصة ، ولكن المراد منه هنا الكلام مطلقاً ، وبعد : فنقول: إن قوله (على الفعل الجميل) يراد به أن يكون الفعل المحمود عليه جميلاً في اعتقاد المحمود بزعم الحامد، سواء أكان جميلاً في النظر العام الصحيح كفعل الخيرات، أم لم يكن جميلا في هذا النظر كنهب الأعمار والأموال في نحو قول القائل:
نَهَبْتَ مِنَ الأَعْمَارِ مَا لَوْ حَوَيْتَهُ لَهُنَّتِ الدُّنْيَا بِأَنَّكَ خَالِدُ وقوله (الاختياري) قيد لإخراج ما هو جميل لكنه حاصل بغير اختيار المحمود وفعله، كطول قامة زيد، وإشراق لونه ؛ فإنّ الثَّناء عليه بمثل ذلك يسمى مدحا ، ولا يسمى حمدا ، واعلم أنَّ المحمود عليه غير المحمود به في الحقيقة والماهية ، لأن المحمود عليه هو الفعل الصادر من المحمود، والمحمود به هو الكلام الصادر من الحامد، ثم قد يتحد مَاصَدَقُهما ذاتا ، ويختلفان اعتبارًا ، كما إذا أكرمك زيد فقلت : زيد كريم ، فإن الكريم من حيث كونه باعنا لك على الحمد يقال له : محمود عليه، ومن حيث كونه مدلول العبارة يقال له: محمود به، وقد يختلفان ذاتا ،واعتبارا ، كما إذا أكرمك زيد فقلت: زيد عالمٌ ، فإنّ المحمود عليه هو الكرم، والمحمود به هو العلم.
Here are four words whose meanings are close, but between each one and the other there is some difference, so it is necessary to define the meaning of each one and relate it to the other, so that their matter becomes fully clear. As for the four words, they are: al-hamd (praise), al-madh (commendation), al-shukr (thanksgiving), and al-thana' (eulogy). As for their meanings: In language, al-hamd is what the commentator mentioned in his saying: 'Vocal eulogy upon the beautiful voluntary act, in a manner of glorification and veneration, whether it is in return for a favor or not.' Before we explain this definition to you, we say to you: In his saying 'vocal eulogy' there is specification of al-hamd to the temporal kind, because verily al-hamd is two types: one of them: temporal al-hamd, which is what occurs from creatures, whether the praised one is eternal like our saying 'Praise be to God', or the praised one is temporal like when you say 'I praised Zayd'; and the second one: eternal al-hamd, which is what is from God Almighty, whether the praised one is eternal like His saying 'Praise be to God', or the praised one is temporal as in His praise of His messengers and His righteous servants. The specification of al-hamd in the definition of the commentator to the temporal kind is because he made it vocal eulogy, and the tongue is the organ of speech for temporal beings only. It can be answered with one of two answers: first: that his intent is the definition of temporal al-hamd, so 'al' in the defined is for specific reference; second: that his intent is the definition of al-hamd absolutely, and his saying 'vocal' does not intend the specific organ, but what is intended here is speech absolutely. After that, we say: his saying 'upon the beautiful act' intends that the act upon which praise is given be beautiful in the belief of the praised according to the praiser, whether it is beautiful in sound general consideration like doing good deeds, or not beautiful in this consideration like plundering of ages and wealth in the poet's saying: 'You plundered from the ages what, if you had possessed it, the world would have congratulated itself that you are eternal.' His saying 'voluntary' is a restriction to exclude what is beautiful but occurs without the choice and action of the praised, such as Zayd's tall stature and the brightness of his complexion; for eulogy upon such is called commendation, and is not called praise. And know that the object of praise (al-mahmud 'alayhi) and the means of praise (al-mahmud bihi) differ in reality and essence, because the object of praise is the action emanating from the praised, and the means of praise is the speech emanating from the praiser. Then they may coincide in referent essentially and differ in consideration, as when Zayd honors you and you say 'Zayd is generous', for the generous, in terms of being a cause for your praise, is called object of praise, and in terms of being the meaning of the expression, is called means of praise. And they may differ both essentially and in consideration, as when Zayd honors you and you say 'Zayd is learned', for the object of praise is generosity, and the means of praise is knowledge.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage defines the linguistic term 'al-hamd' (praise) as part of a discussion of four related terms. The author first introduces the four words: al-hamd, al-madh, al-shukr, and al-thana'. He then presents the definition of al-hamd given by the commentator: 'Vocal eulogy upon the beautiful voluntary act, in a manner of glorification and veneration, whether in return for a favor or not.' The author explains that this definition specifies al-hamd as a temporal act because it uses 'vocal' which refers to the tongue, a temporal organ. He notes that al-hamd can be divided into two types: temporal (from creatures) and eternal (from God). He then addresses a potential objection regarding the use of 'vocal' for God's praise by offering two answers: either the definition is meant for temporal praise specifically, or 'vocal' is interpreted broadly as speech. Next, he clarifies 'beautiful act' as any act deemed beautiful by the praiser, even if not objectively sound. 'Voluntary' excludes involuntary beautiful qualities like height, which would be called commendation not praise. Finally, he distinguishes between the object of praise (the act) and the means of praise (the speech), showing how they can coincide or differ in referent.

Key Terms
الحمد
حادث
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's praise cannot be called al-hamd because it is not vocal.
Correction: God's praise is eternal al-hamd, and the term 'vocal' in the definition can be interpreted broadly as speech or is specific to temporal praise.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of temporal al-hamd with a temporal object?
A) 'Praise be to God' (al-hamdu lillah)
B) 'I praised Zayd'
C) God's praise of His messengers
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Al-hamd is of two types: temporal and eternal; the definition's 'vocal' is either limited to temporal or means speech broadly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say الحمد لله, do you recognize that your praise is temporal and limited, while God's praise of you is eternal and perfect? How does this awareness shape the sincerity of your praise?
?
If 'vocal' can mean speech in a broad sense, what does that imply about the nature of God's praise? Knowing that God praises His servants, are you living in a way that invites His praise?
?
The commentator restricted the definition of al-hamd to temporal praise by mentioning the tongue. In your life, do you limit your worship to what is outward and physical, or do you seek to cultivate an eternal dimension that transcends the tongue?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
7 min
Conditions of Beauty and Voluntariness; Object vs. Means of Praise
Explains the conditions 'beautiful' and 'voluntary' and distinguishes object from means of praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions that make an act praiseworthy (beautiful and voluntary) and the distinction between the object of praise (the act) and the means of praise (the words).

Core Idea
Al-hamd requires a beautiful voluntary act; qualities like height are not praised; also, the praised act and the praising words are distinct entities.

The definition of al-hamd includes two key restrictions: the act must be 'beautiful' and 'voluntary'. This lesson unpacks what these mean, using examples like plundering and natural beauty. It then introduces a subtle distinction between the object of praise (the act that deserves praise) and the means of praise (the words used to praise). Understanding this distinction is crucial for deeper theological discussions.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'beautiful act'
The act praised must be considered beautiful by the praiser, even if it is not objectively good. Example: praising a thief for plundering is still considered praise (al-hamd) if the praiser thinks it beautiful.
This shows al-hamd is subjective from the praiser's perspective.
2
Understand 'voluntary'
The act must be done by choice. Natural qualities like height are not voluntary, so praising them is called commendation (madh), not praise (hamd).
3
Distinguish object and means of praise
The object of praise (mahmud 'alayhi) is the act itself; the means of praise (mahmud bihi) is the speech. They can coincide in referent (e.g., 'generous' refers to the act) or differ (e.g., 'learned' when the act is generosity).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ههنا أربع ألفاظ تتقارب معانيها ، ولكن بين كل واحد منها والآخر تخالفا ما، فوجب أن تحدد معنى كل واحد منها وننسبه إلى الآخر؛ ليتضح أمرها تمام الاتضاح، أما الألفاظ الأربعة فهي : الحمد ، والمدح ، والشكر والثناء). وأما معانيها:

نَهَبْتَ مِنَ الأَعْمَارِ مَا لَوْ حَوَيْتَهُ لَهُنَّتِ الدُّنْيَا بِأَنَّكَ خَالِدُ وقوله (الاختياري) قيد لإخراج ما هو جميل لكنه حاصل بغير اختيار المحمود وفعله، كطول قامة زيد، وإشراق لونه ؛ فإنّ الثَّناء عليه بمثل ذلك يسمى مدحا ، ولا يسمى حمدا ، واعلم أنَّ المحمود عليه غير المحمود به في الحقيقة والماهية ، لأن المحمود عليه هو الفعل الصادر من المحمود، والمحمود به هو الكلام الصادر من الحامد، ثم قد يتحد مَاصَدَقُهما ذاتا ، ويختلفان اعتبارًا ، كما إذا أكرمك زيد فقلت : زيد كريم ، فإن الكريم من حيث كونه باعنا لك على الحمد يقال له : محمود عليه، ومن حيث كونه مدلول العبارة يقال له: محمود به، وقد يختلفان ذاتا ،واعتبارا ، كما إذا أكرمك زيد فقلت: زيد عالمٌ ، فإنّ المحمود عليه هو الكرم، والمحمود به هو العلم.
Here are four words whose meanings are close, but between each one and the other there is some difference, so it is necessary to define the meaning of each one and relate it to the other, so that their matter becomes fully clear. As for the four words, they are: al-hamd (praise), al-madh (commendation), al-shukr (thanksgiving), and al-thana' (eulogy). As for their meanings: In language, al-hamd is what the commentator mentioned in his saying: 'Vocal eulogy upon the beautiful voluntary act, in a manner of glorification and veneration, whether it is in return for a favor or not.' Before we explain this definition to you, we say to you: In his saying 'vocal eulogy' there is specification of al-hamd to the temporal kind, because verily al-hamd is two types: one of them: temporal al-hamd, which is what occurs from creatures, whether the praised one is eternal like our saying 'Praise be to God', or the praised one is temporal like when you say 'I praised Zayd'; and the second one: eternal al-hamd, which is what is from God Almighty, whether the praised one is eternal like His saying 'Praise be to God', or the praised one is temporal as in His praise of His messengers and His righteous servants. The specification of al-hamd in the definition of the commentator to the temporal kind is because he made it vocal eulogy, and the tongue is the organ of speech for temporal beings only. It can be answered with one of two answers: first: that his intent is the definition of temporal al-hamd, so 'al' in the defined is for specific reference; second: that his intent is the definition of al-hamd absolutely, and his saying 'vocal' does not intend the specific organ, but what is intended here is speech absolutely. After that, we say: his saying 'upon the beautiful act' intends that the act upon which praise is given be beautiful in the belief of the praised according to the praiser, whether it is beautiful in sound general consideration like doing good deeds, or not beautiful in this consideration like plundering of ages and wealth in the poet's saying: 'You plundered from the ages what, if you had possessed it, the world would have congratulated itself that you are eternal.' His saying 'voluntary' is a restriction to exclude what is beautiful but occurs without the choice and action of the praised, such as Zayd's tall stature and the brightness of his complexion; for eulogy upon such is called commendation, and is not called praise. And know that the object of praise (al-mahmud 'alayhi) and the means of praise (al-mahmud bihi) differ in reality and essence, because the object of praise is the action emanating from the praised, and the means of praise is the speech emanating from the praiser. Then they may coincide in referent essentially and differ in consideration, as when Zayd honors you and you say 'Zayd is generous', for the generous, in terms of being a cause for your praise, is called object of praise, and in terms of being the meaning of the expression, is called means of praise. And they may differ both essentially and in consideration, as when Zayd honors you and you say 'Zayd is learned', for the object of praise is generosity, and the means of praise is knowledge.

Commentary
This passage defines the linguistic term 'al-hamd' (praise) as part of a discussion of four related terms. The author first introduces the four words: al-hamd, al-madh, al-shukr, and al-thana'. He then presents the definition of al-hamd given by the commentator: 'Vocal eulogy upon the beautiful voluntary act, in a manner of glorification and veneration, whether in return for a favor or not.' He notes that al-hamd can be divided into two types: temporal (from creatures) and eternal (from God). What Is Kalam?

Al-Madh vs Al-Hamd: Shared Ground and Key Difference
Defines al-madh linguistically and compares it with al-hamd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic definition of al-madh and how it differs from al-hamd in terms of scope and conditions.

Core Idea
Al-madh is broader than al-hamd: it includes involuntary beauty and non-intelligent objects.

In this passage, al-Dardīr clarifies the linguistic meaning of al-madh (commendation) and contrasts it with al-hamd (praise). While both involve verbal description of beauty, they differ in key conditions. Understanding this distinction is essential for accurate theological vocabulary.

Guided Path
1
Define al-madh
Read the definition of al-madh as 'describing with beauty upon beauty absolutely' and note what it includes (voluntary/involuntary, intelligent/non-intelligent).
The phrase 'upon beauty' means the description itself is beautiful.
2
Identify shared features
Look for the three points where al-hamd and al-madh agree: both are verbal, both do not require a favor, and both require a beautiful description.
3
Pinpoint the difference
Notice that al-hamd imposes conditions (voluntary act from an intelligent agent) that al-madh does not. This makes al-madh more general.
The relationship is 'absolute generality and specificity' – every hamd is a madh, but not vice versa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المدح لغة فهو : الوصف بالجميل على الجميل مطلقا) نعني أنَّه لا فرق بين أن يكون الجميل الممدوح عليه اختياريًا أم اضطراريًا، وسواء أكان الممدوح من ذوي العلم أم لم يكن ، كما في مدح جوهرة بصفاء لونها ونقائها، وسواء أكانَ في مُقابلة نعمةٍ أم لم يكن ، فالحمد والمدح يشتركان في أنَّ كلا منهما لا يكون إلا بالكلام، وفي أن كلا منهما لا يلزم أن يكون في مقابلة نعمة واصلة من المحمود أو الممدوح إلى الحامد أو المادح، وفي كلا منهما لا يكون إلا بوصف جميل، ويفترقان في أنه يلزم في المحمود عليه أن يكون اختياريًا، وأن يكون صادرًا من ذوي العلم ؛ لأنهم هم الذين يتصور الاختيار منهم؛ فبينَ الحمد والمدح عموم وخصوص مطلق ؛ والمدح أعم.
As for al-madh (commendation) linguistically, it is: describing with beauty upon beauty absolutely, meaning there is no difference between whether the beautiful thing commended is voluntary or involuntary, and whether the commended is among those possessed of intellect or not, as in commending a gem for the purity of its color and its clarity, and whether it is in return for a favor or not. So al-hamd and al-madh share in that each of them is only with speech, and that each of them does not necessarily have to be in return for a favor reaching from the praised/commended to the praiser/commender, and that each of them is only with a beautiful description. They differ in that in al-hamd, what is praised must be voluntary and must emanate from one possessed of intellect, because they are the ones from whom choice can be conceived. So between al-hamd and al-madh there is absolute generality and specificity, and al-madh is more general.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage defines 'al-madh' (commendation) linguistically. It is described as 'describing with beauty upon beauty absolutely', meaning it encompasses any beautiful description, regardless of whether the beautiful attribute is voluntary or involuntary, and regardless of whether the entity commended is intelligent (like a person) or not (like a gem), and regardless of whether it is in response to a favor. The author then compares al-hamd and al-madh. They share three characteristics: both are necessarily verbal, both do not require being in return for a favor, and both are predicated on a beautiful description. They differ in that al-hamd requires the praised act to be voluntary and from an intelligent agent, whereas al-madh does not. Therefore, the relationship between al-hamd and al-madh is one of absolute generality and specificity: every al-hamd is al-madh, but not vice versa. Thus al-madh is the more general term.

Key Terms
المدح
الوصف بالجميل
اختياري
اضطراري
عموم وخصوص مطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-madh is only for intelligent beings like humans.
Correction: The passage states that al-madh can also be for non-intelligent things like a gem's color, as long as the description is beautiful.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between al-hamd and al-madh according to this passage?
A) Al-hamd must be vocal, while al-madh can be non-vocal.
B) Al-hamd requires the praised act to be voluntary, while al-madh does not.
C) Al-madh must be in return for a favor, while al-hamd does not.
D) Al-hamd is only for God, while al-madh is for creatures.

Key Takeaway
Al-madh is a broader term than al-hamd because it includes involuntary and non-intelligent subjects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you praise people for their talents or achievements, do you consider whether those qualities are voluntary (earned by effort) or involuntary (natural gifts)? How does this distinction affect your gratitude to Allah, who is the ultimate giver of all gifts? Commit to specifically praising Allah (hamd) for each blessing you receive today.
?
Reflect on a time you praised a non-intelligent object, like a car or a view. According to this lesson, that praise is al-madh not al-hamd. Does this change how you view the praise you give to created things? Will you now reserve your deepest hamd for Allah alone, and be more mindful of the type of praise you offer to others?
?
The passage says al-madh does not require a favor in return. Think about your own compliments: do you often expect something back? How can you purify your praise to be more like hamd—given freely, without expecting a return, and directed only to where it is truly deserved? Write one specific way you will adjust your speech today.

The Linguistic Definition of Al-Shukr and Its Comparison with Al-Hamd and Al-Madh
Defines al-shukr linguistically and clarifies its relationship with al-hamd and al-madh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic definition of al-shukr and how it relates to al-hamd and al-madh in terms of generality and specificity.

Core Idea
Al-shukr is broader in form but narrower in cause than praise.

In this lesson, we explore the linguistic definition of shukr (thanksgiving) and how it compares to two related terms: hamd (praise) and madh (laudation). The author explains that shukr is not limited to words; it includes beliefs and actions, but it is only given in response to a favor. Understanding these nuances helps us use these terms precisely in theology.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition of shukr
Read the definition: shukr is an action indicating glorification of the benefactor. Note that 'action' (فعل) is broader than just speech.
This sets the foundational meaning.
2
Understand the breadth of 'action'
The author clarifies that 'action' includes speech, belief, and bodily deeds. This makes shukr more general in form than pure verbal praise.
Contrast with hamd and madh which are only verbal.
3
Analyze the two-way relationship
Shukr is more specific than hamd/madh because it requires a favor in return. Some instances of hamd/madh are shukr (if verbal and in return for a favor), and some shukr is not hamd/madh (if non-verbal).
This shows partial overlap, not full equivalence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا الشُّكر لغة فهو: (فعل) يُنبئ عن تعظيم المُنعِم من حيث كونه منعما)، والمراد بالفعل: هو أعم من القول باللسان والاعتقاد بالجنان والعمل بالأركان التي هي الجوارح، ومن هذا التعميم مع قولنا (تعظيم المنعم) مع أن المنعم هو موصول الإحسان يتضح لك أن الشكر أعم من كل من المدح والحمد من جهة ، وأخصُّ من كل منهما من جهة أخرى ، وبيان ذلك أن الشكر يكون قولاً واعتقاداً وفعلا من أفعال الجوارح، في حين أن الحمد والمدح لا يكون واحد منهما إلا كلاماً ، فهذه جهة عموم الشُّكر ، وأنَّ الشُّكر لا يكون إلا في مقابلة نعمة صادرة من المشكور سواء أكانت عائدة على الشاكر أم على غيره ، في حين أنَّ الحمد والمدح لا يلزم فيهما ذلك، فهذه جهة خصوص الشكر ؛ فبين الحمد والمدح مع الشكر عموم وخصوص وجهي؛ فبعض الحمدِ وبعض المدح يكون شكرًا؛ وهو ما يكون بالكلام في مقابلة نعمة، وبعض الحمد وبعض المدح ليس شكرًا وهو ما يكون بالكلام في غير مقابلة نعمة ، وبعض الشكر لا حمدا ولا مدحاً يسمى ما يكون عملا وهو من أعمال الجوارح في مقابلة نعمة .
As for al-shukr (thanksgiving) linguistically, it is: an action that indicates the glorification of the benefactor in terms of his being a benefactor. What is meant by 'action' is more general than speech with the tongue, belief with the heart, and work with the limbs which are the organs. From this generalization along with our saying 'glorification of the benefactor' despite the benefactor being the one who bestows beneficence, it becomes clear to you that al-shukr is more general than both al-madh and al-hamd in one respect, and more specific than each of them in another respect. The explanation of that is that al-shukr can be speech, belief, or an act of the limbs, while neither al-hamd nor al-madh is anything but speech. So this is the respect in which al-shukr is more general. And that al-shukr only occurs in return for a favor emanating from the thanked, whether it reverts to the thanker or to another, while al-hamd and al-madh do not necessarily require that. So this is the respect in which al-shukr is more specific. Thus between al-hamd and al-madh on one hand and al-shukr on the other, there is a two-way generality and specificity: some al-hamd and some al-madh are al-shukr – that which is speech in return for a favor; and some al-hamd and some al-madh are not al-shukr – that which is speech not in return for a favor; and some al-shukr is neither al-hamd nor al-madh – that which is an action, which is from the acts of the limbs in return for a favor.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage defines 'al-shukr' (thanksgiving) linguistically. Al-shukr is an action (فعل) that indicates glorification of the benefactor. 'Action' here is broad: it includes verbal praise, inner belief, and physical deeds. The author then compares al-shukr to al-hamd and al-madh. Al-shukr is more general in that it can be done by speech, belief, or action, while both al-hamd and al-madh are only verbal. However, al-shukr is more specific in that it is only in response to a favor (from the benefactor), whereas al-hamd and al-madh do not require a favor. Therefore, the relationship between al-shukr and the other two is a two-way relationship of generality and specificity: some instances of hamd/madh are shukr (if verbal and in return for a favor), some are not shukr (if not in return for a favor), and some shukr is not hamd/madh (if it is a non-verbal action done in return for a favor). This shows a partial overlap.

Key Takeaway
Al-shukr is a comprehensive act (speech, belief, deed) done in return for a favor, distinguishing it from general praise.

Customary Hamd, Shukr, and the Scope of Thana'
Defines customary hamd and shukr, then linguistic thana'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will distinguish between the customary meanings of al-hamd and al-shukr, and understand the linguistic definition of al-thana' as the most general term for expressions of glorification.

Core Idea
Al-thana' is the most inclusive form of praise, encompassing words, beliefs, and actions.

After establishing the linguistic meanings of praise terms, the author now turns to their customary (urfi) usages, which often shift in nuance. He clarifies the relationship between customary hamd and linguistic shukr, then defines shukr in its customary sense as the proper use of blessings. Finally, he introduces al-thana' as a broader category that includes any action indicating glorification.

Guided Path
1
Understand the customary relationship
The author states that customary hamd is identical to linguistic shukr, implying a two-way generality-specificity relationship previously explained. Identify this relationship as a key nuance.
This shows that definitions can shift when moving from linguistic to customary usage.
2
Learn the customary definition of shukr
Customary shukr is defined as the servant directing all of God's blessings to the purpose for which they were created. This is an active, practical gratitude.
Note that this is more than verbal praise; it involves proper use of blessings.
3
Grasp the linguistic definition of thana'
Thana' is an action that indicates glorification of the one eulogized, and it can be speech, belief, or physical deed. Compare its generality with hamd and madh.
The text cuts off but the point is clear: thana' is more general because it includes action beyond speech.
4
Review and compare all three terms
Use the definitions to create a mental comparison: customary hamd ≈ linguistic shukr; customary shukr is practical gratitude; thana' is the broadest, covering all expressions of glorification.

Source Text

Comparing Hamd, Madh, Shukr, and Thana
The author linguistically defines and compares hamd, madh, shukr, and thana.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise linguistic definitions of hamd, madh, shukr, and thana, and how they relate to each other in terms of generality and specificity.

Core Idea
Hamd, madh, shukr, and thana are not synonyms; each has a unique scope and conditions.

This passage from the Itḥāf al-Murīd begins by defining key terms related to praise and thankfulness. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly interpreting Islamic texts and avoiding common confusions. The author carefully compares the terms, showing where they overlap and where they differ.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions carefully
Read the Arabic and translation for each term: madh, hamd, shukr, and thana. Note the specific conditions mentioned for each.
The passage ends incomplete; the definition of thana may continue in the next lesson.
2
Identify shared features
Notice what hamd and madh have in common: both require speech and beautiful description, and neither requires a favor in return.
3
Spot the differences
See how hamd requires the praised quality to be voluntary and from a rational being, while madh does not.
This is the key distinction: madh is more general.
4
Understand the relationships
Follow the author's explanation of 'absolute generality and specificity' (عموم وخصوص مطلق) between hamd and madh, and 'generality and specificity from one perspective' (عموم وخصوص وجهي) between shukr and the others.
Shukr is more general in modes (includes actions) but more specific in requiring a favor.

Source Text

وأما المدح لغةً فهو : الوصف بالجميل على الجميل مطلقا) نعني أنَّه لا فرق بين أن يكون الجميل الممدوح عليه اختياريًا أم اضطراريًا، وسواء أكان الممدوح من ذوي العلم أم لم يكن ، كما في مدح جوهرة بصفاء لونها ونقائها، وسواء أكانَ في مُقابلة نعمةٍ أم لم يكن ، فالحمد والمدح يشتركان في أنَّ كلا منهما لا يكون إلا بالكلام، وفي أن كلا منهما لا يلزم أن يكون في مقابلة نعمة واصلة من المحمود أو الممدوح إلى الحامد أو المادح، وفي كلا منهما لا يكون إلا بوصف جميل، ويفترقان في أنه يلزم في المحمود عليه أن يكون اختياريًا، وأن يكون صادرًا من ذوي العلم ؛ لأنهم هم الذين يتصور الاختيار منهم؛ فبينَ الحمد والمدح عموم وخصوص مطلق ؛ والمدح أعم. وأمَّا الشُّكر لغة فهو: (فعل) يُنبئ عن تعظيم المُنعِم من حيث كونه منعما)، والمراد بالفعل: هو أعم من القول باللسان والاعتقاد بالجنان والعمل بالأركان التي هي الجوارح، ومن هذا التعميم مع قولنا (تعظيم المنعم) مع أن المنعم هو موصول الإحسان يتضح لك أن الشكر أعم من كل من المدح والحمد من جهة ، وأخصُّ من كل منهما من جهة أخرى ، وبيان ذلك أن الشكر يكون قولاً واعتقاداً وفعلا من أفعال الجوارح ، في حين أن الحمد والمدح لا يكون واحد منهما إلا كلاماً ، فهذه جهة عموم الشُّكر ، وأنَّ الشُّكر لا يكون إلا في مقابلة نعمة صادرة من المشكور سواء أكانت عائدة على الشاكر أم على غيره ، في حين أنَّ الحمد والمدح لا يلزم فيهما ذلك، فهذه جهة خصوص الشكر ؛ فبين الحمد والمدح مع الشكر عموم وخصوص وجهي؛ فبعض الحمدِ وبعض المدح يكون شكرًا؛ وهو ما يكون بالكلام في مقابلة نعمة، وبعض الحمد وبعض المدح ليس شكرًا وهو ما يكون بالكلام في غير مقابلة نعمة ، وبعض الشكر لا حمدا ولا مدحاً يسمى ما يكون عملا وهو من أعمال الجوارح في مقابلة نعمة . بین الحمد اللغوي، وقد اشتهر أنَّ الحمد في العُرف هو نفس الشكر اللغوي، فتكون النسبة والحمد العرفي العموم والخصوص الوجهي على نحو ما عرفت. والشكر عرفا هو: صرف العبد جميع ما أنعم الله عليه به فيما خُلق لأجله. وأما الثَّناء لغة فهو : فعل ما يُشعر بتعظيم المثنى عليه ، فهو أعمُّ الجميع أن يكون قولاً أو اعتقادا أو عملا بالجارحة ؛ فهو من هذه الناحية أعم من الحمد والمدح ، = لأنَّ الفعل أعم من
Discussion on the basmala and the hamdala and what pertains to them. What is that? And as for praise (madh) linguistically: it is describing with beauty upon beauty absolutely, meaning that there is no difference between the beautiful thing praised being voluntary or involuntary, whether the praised one is of those who possess knowledge or not, as in praising a jewel for the purity and clarity of its colour, and whether it is in return for a favor or not. So hamd and madh share in that each of them is only with speech, and that each of them does not necessarily have to be in return for a favor reaching from the praised one to the praiser, and that each is only with a beautiful description. They differ in that in the one praised, what is praised must be voluntary and must emanate from those who possess knowledge, because they are the ones from whom choice is conceivable. Thus between hamd and madh there is absolute generality and specificity, and madh is more general. As for thankfulness (shukr) linguistically: it is an act that indicates magnifying the benefactor inasmuch as he is a benefactor. What is meant by act is more general than speech with the tongue, belief in the heart, and action with the limbs which are the physical faculties. From this generalization along with our saying 'magnifying the Benefactor' while the Benefactor is the one who conveys beneficence, it becomes clear to you that shukr is more general than both madh and hamd in one respect, and more specific than each of them in another respect. Explanation: shukr can be speech, belief, and action of the limbs, whereas hamd and madh are only speech—this is the aspect of shukr's generality. And shukr only occurs in return for a favor emanating from the thanked one, whether that favor benefits the thanker or someone else, whereas hamd and madh do not necessarily require that—this is the aspect of shukr's specificity. Thus between hamd and madh on one hand and shukr on the other there is generality and specificity from one perspective (wajhī): some hamd and some madh are shukr—that which is with speech in return for a favor—and some hamd and some madh are not shukr—that which is with speech not in return for a favor—and some shukr is neither hamd nor madh, called that which is action, i.e., actions of the limbs in return for a favor. Between linguistic hamd, and it is well-known that hamd in common usage is the same as linguistic shukr, so the relationship and customary hamd are generality and specificity from one perspective as you have understood. And shukr customarily is: the servant's expenditure of all that Allah has bestowed upon him in that for which it was created. As for eulogy (thanā') linguistically: it is an act that indicates magnifying the one eulogized. It is the most general of all, being speech, belief, or action with the limb. So from this aspect it is more general than hamd and madh, because the act is more general than
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage defines and compares four key terms: hamd (praise), madh (eulogy), shukr (thanksgiving), and thanā' (eulogy/glorification). The author begins by defining madh linguistically as describing something beautiful with beauty, regardless of whether the beauty is voluntary or involuntary, and whether it is in response to a favor. He then compares hamd and madh, noting that both require speech and do not require a favor, but differ in that hamd requires the praised quality to be voluntary and from a rational being. Madh is more general. Next, shukr is defined as an act indicating magnification of the benefactor. The author explains that shukr is more general than hamd and madh because it includes speech, belief, and action, but more specific because it only occurs in return for a favor. He then gives the relationship as 'generality and specificity from one perspective' (wajhī). The customary meaning of shukr is also given: using all of Allah's gifts for their intended purpose. Finally, thanā' is defined as an act indicating magnification, and is the most general because it includes all three modes and does not require a favor. The passage ends with the incomplete sentence 'because the act is more general than', which likely continues on the next page.

شكر

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hamd and shukr are synonyms and can be used interchangeably.
Correction: The passage shows that hamd and shukr differ in scope: hamd is only speech with a beautiful description and does not require a favor, while shukr includes actions and requires a favor. They have a relationship of generality and specificity from one perspective (wajhī).

?

Salat means mercy from Allah, seeking forgiveness from angels, and supplication from humans.

(ثُمَّ سَلَامُ الله) (۱) أي: تحيَّتَهُ اللَّائِقَةُ به ، بحسب ما عنده تعالى.
من
(مع صَلَاتِه) أي : رحمتِه المقرُونَةِ بالتَّعظيم، أو مُطلقها، والصلاة الله : الرَّحمةُ ، ومِن الملائكة: الاستغفارُ ، ومن الآدميين: التضرع والدُّعاء (۲).
=
ولم يُشترط فيه أن يكون في مقابلة نعمة ; فهو مِن هذه الناحية أعم من الشكر
Discussion on the prayer and salutation upon the Prophet and what pertains to them. And technically: an act that indicates magnifying the Benefactor because of His being a Benefactor, whether that act is belief in the heart, or speech with the tongue, or action with the limbs and organs. Then the salam of Allah—i.e., His greeting befitting Him, according to what He has, exalted be He. With His salat—i.e., His mercy accompanied by magnification, or mercy absolutely. And salat from Allah is mercy, from angels is seeking forgiveness, and from humans is supplication and prayer. And it was not conditioned that it be in return for a favor; so from this aspect it is more general than shukr.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage provides definitions for salam and salat upon the Prophet. It begins with a technical definition of shukr (thanā'?) as an act indicating magnification of the Benefactor. Then it defines salam as Allah's greeting befitting His majesty. The phrase 'with His salat' is explained as His mercy accompanied by magnification. The author then distinguishes the meaning of salat depending on the agent: from Allah it is mercy, from angels it is seeking forgiveness, and from humans it is supplication and prayer. Finally, it notes that this (probably thanā') is not conditioned on being in return for a favor, making it more general than shukr.

Key Terms
سلام
صلاة
رحمة
استغفار
تضرع
دعاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think salat from humans is the same as from Allah.
Correction: The passage clarifies that salat from humans is supplication and prayer, whereas from Allah it is mercy.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of salat from angels according to the passage?
A) Mercy
B) Supplication
C) Seeking forgiveness (istighfar)
D) Greeting

Key Takeaway
The term 'salat' has different meanings depending on who is sending it: mercy from Allah, seeking forgiveness from angels, and supplication from humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Knowing that your 'salat' upon the Prophet is a supplication, not a conferral of mercy, does it not make you more humble and reliant on Allah? Will you now pause before each 'salli' to mean it as a genuine request?
?
The angels' salat is istighfar—they seek forgiveness for the Prophet. Does your own salat include seeking forgiveness for him and the believers? What specific du'a will you add today?
