The Definition of Thana' and Its Relation to Shukr
Technical definition of thana' and its comparison to shukr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of thanā' (praise) as an act magnifying the Benefactor, and how it differs from shukr.

Core Idea
Thana' is magnifying the Benefactor unconditionally, unlike shukr which is in response to a favor.

Before defining the salat and salam, the author presents a general definition of thanā' (praise) as an act that indicates magnifying the Benefactor. This concept is foundational for understanding Islamic theological terminology. In this lesson, we explore that definition and its relation to shukr.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of thana'
Read the Arabic definition carefully. Note that it is a 'f'l' (act) that indicates magnification of the Benefactor.
2
Identify the three modes
The definition includes three ways this act can be performed: belief in the heart, speech with the tongue, and action with the limbs. Find these in the text.
This shows thana' is comprehensive.
3
Compare with shukr
The passage ends by noting that thana' is more general than shukr because it is not conditioned on receiving a favor. Understand the difference.
Shukr is gratitude for a specific blessing.

Linguistic Derivations of 'Nabi'
Author enumerates the linguistic derivations of 'nabi' and 'nabi''.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible linguistic derivations of the word 'nabī' and how they influence its meaning.

Core Idea
The word 'nabī' can be derived from 'naba' (news) or 'nubuwwa' (elevation), each offering insights into prophethood.

In this passage, the author examines the etymology of the word 'nabī'. He shows that it can be traced to two different roots, each giving a different but complementary understanding of what it means to be a prophet. Let's explore these derivations and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage about the derivations of 'nabī' and 'nabī''. Then read the literal translation to grasp the meanings.
Notice the two pronunciations: 'nabī' with shadda and 'nabī'' with hamza.
2
Identify the two derivations
The passage explains the derivation of 'nabī'' (with hamza) and then 'nabī' (with shadda). For each, note the roots: 'naba' and 'nubuwwa', and how they lead to active/passive meanings.
3
Reflect on the meanings
Consider how each derivation highlights a different aspect of prophethood: the prophet as informer (active) or informed (passive) from 'naba', and the prophet as one who elevates or is elevated from 'nubuwwa'.
These complementary meanings enrich our understanding of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقال (نَبِيُّ) بتشديد الياء، ويقال (نبيء) بالهمزة في آخره، فأما المهموز فلا يحتمل في الاشتقاق إلا وجها واحدا ، وهو أن يكون أصله النبأ وهو الخبر ، والنبيء: فعيل منه إما بمعنى الفاعل ؛ لأنه المخبر عن الله تعالى إن كان مع نبوته رسولا ، ولأنه يخبر عن حال نفسه لكي يحترمه الناس إن لم يكون رسولا ، أو بمعنى المفعول لأنَّ مَلَكَ الوحي يخبره. فأَمَّا النَّبِيُّ بتشديد الياء فيحتمل في الاشتقاق وجهين أولهما أن يكون من النبأ، وأصله على هذا الوجه نبئ ، فسُهلتِ الهمزة بقلبها ياء ثم أُدغمت الياء في الياء، ويأتي فيه الوجهان المذكوران، وثانيهما: أنَّ أصله من النبوَةِ - بفتح فسكون - بمعنى الارتفاع ، وأصله نَبِيوِّ ، على زنة عَلِيمٌ ، فلما اجتمعت الواو والياء، وسبقت إحداهما بالسكون قلبت الواو ياء، وأُدْغِمت الياء في الياء. ويحتمل ـ على هذا أيضا ـ أن يكون فعيلا بمعنى فاعل لأنه رافع لشأن من اتبعه على غيره ممن لم يتبعه، وأن يكون بمعنى مفعول لأنه مرفوع المنزلة ، وإنما المصنف بـ: (نَبِيَّ) دون (رسول) لأمرين: الأول: موافقة الآية الكريمة: ﴿إِنَّ الله وَمَلَته كَتَهُ يُصَلُّونَ عَلَى النّبي [الأحزاب: ٥٦] ، والثاني: الإشارة إلى أنه يستحق الصلاة والسلام عليه بعنوان النُّبوة التي هي أعم من الرّسالة ؛ فيكون مستحقا لهما بعنوان الرسالة من باب الأولى ؛ فإنَّ الرِّسالة حيثُ وجِدَتْ ؛ وجدتِ النُّبوة، ولا عكس.
It is said (nabī) with tashdīd of the yā', and it is said (nabī’) with hamza at its end. As for the one with hamza, it only admits one aspect in derivation: that its origin is al-naba’ meaning news, and al-nabī’ is fa‘īl from it, either in the sense of the active participle because he is the one who informs about Allah the Exalted if he is a messenger along with his prophethood, and because he informs about his own state so that people may honour him if he is not a messenger, or in the sense of the passive participle because the angel of revelation informs him. As for al-nabī with tashdīd of the yā’, it admits two aspects in derivation: first, that it is from al-naba’, its origin on this aspect is nabi’, then the hamza was softened by turning it into a yā’ and then the yā’ was assimilated into the yā’, and the two mentioned aspects apply to it; second, that its origin is from al-nubuwwa – with a fatḥa then sukūn – meaning elevation, and its origin is nabiyw, on the measure of ‘alīm, and when the wāw and yā’ came together and one of them was preceded by sukūn, the wāw was turned into a yā’ and the yā’ was assimilated into the yā’. It also admits – on this too – that it could be fa‘īl in the sense of the active participle because he elevates the status of those who follow him over others who do not follow him, and that it could be in the sense of the passive participle because he is of elevated rank. The author used (nabī) instead of (rasūl) for two reasons: first, in accordance with the noble verse: “Indeed, Allah and His angels invoke blessings upon the Prophet” [al-Aḥzāb 56]; second, to indicate that he deserves ṣalāh and salām upon him by virtue of prophethood, which is more general than messengership; thus he deserves them by virtue of messengership a fortiori, for messengership wherever it exists, prophethood exists, but not the converse.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic and terminological analysis explaining why the author chose the word 'nabī' over 'rasūl' in the line 'alā nabiyyin jā'a bi-l-tawḥīd'. The author first presents the two possible pronunciations: 'nabī' with a shadda on the yā', and 'nabī'' with a hamza. He then examines the etymology of each. The version with hamza can only be derived from 'naba' (news), and 'nabī'' can be interpreted either as an active participle (the one who informs) or a passive participle (the one who is informed). The version with shadda on the yā' has two possible derivations: either it is a softened form of the hamza version, thus carrying the same two meanings, or it is from 'nubuwwa' (elevation), in which case it can also mean either the one who elevates (active) or the one who is elevated (passive). The author then gives two reasons for preferring 'nabī' over 'rasūl': first, to match the Qur'anic verse in Sūrat al-Aḥzāb; second, to indicate that the Prophet deserves blessings by virtue of prophethood, which is more general than messengership, making the entitlement a fortiori. The note ends by clarifying the relationship: wherever there is messengership, there is prophethood, but not vice versa.

Key Terms
نبي
نبأ
النبوة
فعيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'nabī' and 'nabī'' are completely different words with unrelated meanings.
Correction: Both are valid pronunciations; 'nabī' can be traced to the same root 'naba' through phonetic softening, or to a different root 'nubuwwa'. They are related but offer different nuances.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the only possible origin for the word 'nabī'' (with hamza)?
A) It is derived from 'nubuwwa' meaning elevation.
B) It is derived from 'naba' meaning news.
C) It is derived from 'rasūl' meaning messenger.
D) It has no clear origin.

Key Takeaway
The word 'nabī' has deep linguistic roots that reveal prophethood as both informing and elevating, active and passive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You've just learned that 'nabī' can mean both 'one who informs' (active) and 'one who is informed' (passive). Which aspect of prophethood do you need to embrace more in your own life: actively conveying truth to others, or being receptive to guidance from above? What one step will you take this week to live that out?
?
The word 'nabī' also conveys elevation—both elevating others and being elevated. Do you see yourself as someone who lifts others through your actions, or do you expect to be elevated by others without effort? How can you actively elevate at least one person today, without seeking recognition?
?
The passage shows that the very word for prophet is rich with meaning, not arbitrary. When you speak about prophethood, do you treat it as a deep reality or just a label? How can you ensure your understanding of prophets goes beyond mere titles and becomes a transformative conviction?

Why Nabī is Chosen Over Rasūl
Author gives two reasons for preferring 'nabī' over 'rasūl'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two reasons the author gives for using 'nabī' instead of 'rasūl' in his poem.

Core Idea
The author prefers 'nabī' because it is broader and aligns with Qur'anic usage, emphasizing that prophethood includes messengership.

After examining the linguistic roots of 'nabī', the author explains his choice of this word over 'rasūl' in the line of his poem. He provides two clear reasons: one based on a Qur'anic verse, and another based on the logical relationship between prophethood and messengership. Let's explore these reasons.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Focus on the final part of the passage where the author gives the two reasons. Read it in Arabic and English.
2
Identify the two reasons
The author lists two reasons. List them: 1) Following the Qur'anic verse in Surah al-Ahzab; 2) To indicate that the Prophet deserves blessings by virtue of prophethood, which is more general.
The second reason involves a logical a fortiori argument.
3
Understand the relationship
The passage explains that prophethood is more general than messengership: every messenger is a prophet, but not every prophet is a messenger. Therefore, using 'nabī' covers both statuses.
This is why 'nabī' is preferred: it is broader and emphasizes the comprehensive rank.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقال (نَبِيُّ) بتشديد الياء، ويقال (نبيء) بالهمزة في آخره، فأما المهموز فلا يحتمل في الاشتقاق إلا وجها واحدا ، وهو أن يكون أصله النبأ وهو الخبر ، والنبيء: فعيل منه إما بمعنى الفاعل ؛ لأنه المخبر عن الله تعالى إن كان مع نبوته رسولا ، ولأنه يخبر عن حال نفسه لكي يحترمه الناس إن لم يكون رسولا ، أو بمعنى المفعول لأنَّ مَلَكَ الوحي يخبره. فأَمَّا النَّبِيُّ بتشديد الياء فيحتمل في الاشتقاق وجهين أولهما أن يكون من النبأ، وأصله على هذا الوجه نبئ ، فسُهلتِ الهمزة بقلبها ياء ثم أُدغمت الياء في الياء، ويأتي فيه الوجهان المذكوران، وثانيهما: أنَّ أصله من النبوَةِ - بفتح فسكون - بمعنى الارتفاع ، وأصله نَبِيوِّ ، على زنة عَلِيمٌ ، فلما اجتمعت الواو والياء، وسبقت إحداهما بالسكون قلبت الواو ياء، وأُدْغِمت الياء في الياء. ويحتمل ـ على هذا أيضا ـ أن يكون فعيلا بمعنى فاعل لأنه رافع لشأن من اتبعه على غيره ممن لم يتبعه، وأن يكون بمعنى مفعول لأنه مرفوع المنزلة ، وإنما المصنف بـ: (نَبِيَّ) دون (رسول) لأمرين: الأول: موافقة الآية الكريمة: ﴿إِنَّ الله وَمَلَته كَتَهُ يُصَلُّونَ عَلَى النّبي [الأحزاب: ٥٦] ، والثاني: الإشارة إلى أنه يستحق الصلاة والسلام عليه بعنوان النُّبوة التي هي أعم من الرّسالة ؛ فيكون مستحقا لهما بعنوان الرسالة من باب الأولى ؛ فإنَّ الرِّسالة حيثُ وجِدَتْ ؛ وجدتِ النُّبوة، ولا عكس.

Misconception: Many think 'nabī' and 'rasūl' are interchangeable synonyms.
Correction: They are distinct: a 'rasūl' is a 'nabī' who receives a new law, while a 'nabī' is a broader category. The author chooses 'nabī' for its comprehensiveness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the second reason for using 'nabī' instead of 'rasūl'?
A) Because 'rasūl' is not a Qur'anic term.
B) To indicate that prophethood is more general than messengership.
C) Because 'nabī' is easier to pronounce.
D) To avoid confusion with the word 'rasūl'.

Key Takeaway
The author's use of 'nabī' over 'rasūl' emphasizes the comprehensive nature of prophethood and follows the Qur'anic precedent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you send blessings upon the Prophet (ṣallallāhu 'alayhi wa sallam), do you consciously acknowledge him as both nabī and rasūl, recognizing that his prophethood encompasses all aspects of his mission? Examine your own practice: do you limit your reverence to only his role as a messenger, or do you embrace the full breadth of his prophethood as this lesson teaches? What change can you make today to honor his prophethood more fully?
?
The author chooses 'nabī' over 'rasūl' because it is broader and follows the Qur'an. In your own life, when you have a choice between a broader, more inclusive understanding of Islam (like prophethood) and a narrower one (like messengership), which do you lean towards? Are you willing to follow the Qur'anic precedence even when it challenges your personal preference? Identify one area where you have favored a narrow view and commit to adopting the broader one.
?
Consider the a fortiori argument: if the Prophet deserves blessings as a prophet, then certainly as a messenger. How does this principle of 'from greater to lesser' apply to your own obedience? If you can obey Allah in broader commands, shouldn't you also obey in narrower ones? What specific area of your life are you neglecting because you think it's 'extra' or 'less important'? Write down one neglected act of worship and resolve to practice it this week.

Defining Shar‘ī Tawḥīd: Singling Out God with Worship
Author defines shar‘ī tawḥīd as exclusive worship with belief in oneness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core definition of shar‘ī tawḥīd as exclusive worship combined with belief in oneness.

Core Idea
Shar‘ī tawḥīd means directing all worship to Allah alone while affirming His oneness.

This lesson unpacks the very first definition of tawḥīd given in the text. The author distinguishes between the linguistic meaning and the legal/religious meaning, grounding theology in both action and belief. You'll see how the definition immediately links worship to creed.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without translation. Notice the key phrase 'إفراد المعبود بالعبادة' and the conjunction 'مع اعتقاد'.
The phrase 'مع اعتقاد' shows that worship alone is not enough; correct belief is also required.
2
Identify the two components of the definition
The definition has two parts: (1) singling out the worshipped with worship (action), and (2) believing in His oneness (belief). Identify each part in the text.
'إفراد' signifies exclusivity; 'مع اعتقاد' adds the cognitive dimension.
3
Contrast with mere verbal profession
Ask yourself: why is it not enough to just say 'there is no god but Allah'? The definition requires both worship and belief. Reflect on how this combats hypocrisy.
This connects to the common misunderstanding mentioned in the source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان معنى التوحيد
(بالتَّوْحِيدِ) الشَّرعيّ ، وهو : إفراد المعبودِ بالعبادة، مع اعتقاد وَحْدَته ذاتاً ، وصفاتٍ ، وأفعالا ، فلا تقبل ذاته الانقسام بوجهٍ ، ولا تُشبه ذاتُه الذَّواتِ ، ولا تُشبه صفاتُه الصِّفاتِ ، ولا يدخل أفعاله الاشتراك .
Clarification of the meaning of tawḥīd. (al-tawḥīd) al-shar‘ī: it is singling out the One worshipped with worship, along with belief in His oneness in essence, attributes, and actions. So His essence does not admit division in any way, nor does His essence resemble essences, nor do His attributes resemble attributes, nor does partnership enter into His actions.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a definition of shar‘ī tawḥīd, i.e., the legal/religious meaning of tawḥīd as distinct from its linguistic meaning. The author defines it as 'singling out the One worshipped with worship' (i‘rād al-ma‘būd bi-l-‘ibādah) while also holding the belief in His oneness in three domains: essence (dhāt), attributes (ṣifāt), and actions (af‘āl). The definition then elaborates on what this oneness entails: the essence cannot be divided in any way (negating composition or parts), the essence does not resemble any other essence (negating anthropomorphism), the attributes do not resemble any other attributes (negating any likeness in attributes), and no partnership or sharing enters into His actions (negating any associates in His acts). This definition covers the essential aspects of tawḥīd as understood in Sunni theology: tawḥīd al-ulūhiyyah (worship), tawḥīd al-dhāt (essence), tawḥīd al-ṣifāt (attributes), and tawḥīd al-af‘āl (actions).

Key Terms
التوحيد الشرعي
إفراد المعبود بالعبادة
اعتقد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think tawḥīd is only affirming Allah's existence or just saying 'La ilaha illa Allah' without the requirement of exclusive worship and comprehensive belief.
Correction: This definition clarifies that tawḥīd requires both exclusive worship (إفراد بالعبادة) and belief in oneness (اعتقاد الوحدة).

Checkpoint
According to the definition, what are the two components of shar‘ī tawḥīd?
A) Belief in God and charity
B) Singling out God with worship and belief in His oneness
C) Fasting and prayer
D) Knowledge of Arabic and theology

Key Takeaway
Shar‘ī tawḥīd is both exclusive worship and belief in Allah's oneness in essence, attributes, and actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you worship Allah, do you find any part of your heart seeking the approval of people? How does the demand to 'single out the One worshipped with worship' force you to examine every intention, even in hidden acts?
?
If you truly believed that Allah is one in essence, attributes, and actions, how would that change the way you trust Him in difficult moments? What attribute of His do you most need to internalize today?
?
After this lesson, what specific practice or belief about Allah do you realize you have been neglecting? Commit now to one concrete step to align your worship and belief with this definition of tawḥīd.

The Three Domains of Tawḥīd: Essence, Attributes, and Actions
Author elaborates on the oneness of essence, attributes, and actions through three negations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what it means to affirm Allah's oneness in essence, attributes, and actions, and the negations this entails.

Core Idea
Tawḥīd means negating division, resemblance, and partnership in Allah's essence, attributes, and actions.

After defining tawḥīd in action and belief, the author elaborates on the three categories of oneness: essence, attributes, and actions. This lesson unpacks the three negations that define this oneness: no division, no resemblance, no partnership. Each negation safeguards a different aspect of tawḥīd.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read from 'فلا تقبل ذاته الانقسام' to the end. Notice the three negations introduced by 'لا'.
Each negation corresponds to one of the three domains: essence, attributes, actions.
2
Match each negation to its domain
Identify which negation applies to essence, which to attributes, and which to actions. Check the Arabic: 'ذاته' for essence, 'صفاته' for attributes, 'أفعاله' for actions.
'فلا تقبل ذاته الانقسام' negates division (essence); 'ولا تُشبه ذاتُه الذَّواتِ' negates resemblance (essence again? Wait careful: 'ولا تُشبه ذاتُه الذَّواتِ' is also about essence? Actually the text says: 'فلا تقبل ذاته الانقسام بوجهٍ ، ولا تُشبه ذاتُه الذَّواتِ ، ولا تُشبه صفاتُه الصِّفاتِ ، ولا يدخل أفعاله الاشتراك'. So first two are about essence, then attributes, then actions. So essence has two negations: no division and no resemblance. So the mapping: essence (no division, no resemblance), attributes (no resemblance), actions (no partnership).
3
Understand the theological stakes
Consider why each negation is important. Negating division denies any physical composition (rejecting anthropomorphism). Negating resemblance upholds tanzīh (transcendence). Negating partnership guards against shirk in actions (e.g., believing others create or provide).
Each negation protects a core Sunni doctrine.

Source Text

(بالتَّوْحِيدِ) الشَّرعيّ ، وهو : إفراد المعبودِ بالعبادة، مع اعتقاد وَحْدَته ذاتاً ، وصفاتٍ ، وأفعالا ، فلا تقبل ذاته الانقسام بوجهٍ ، ولا تُشبه ذاتُه الذَّواتِ ، ولا تُشبه صفاتُه الصِّفاتِ ، ولا يدخل أفعاله الاشتراك .

So His essence does not admit division in any way, nor does His essence resemble essences, nor do His attributes resemble attributes, nor does partnership enter into His actions.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a definition of shar‘ī tawḥīd, i.e., the legal/religious meaning of tawḥīd as distinct from its linguistic meaning. The author defines it as 'singling out the One worshipped with worship' (i‘rād al-ma‘būd bi-l-‘ibādah) while also holding the belief in His oneness in three domains: essence (dhāt), attributes (ṣifāt), and actions (af‘āl). The definition then elaborates on what this oneness entails: the essence cannot be divided in any way (negating composition or parts), the essence does not resemble any other essence (negating anthropomorphism), the attributes do not resemble any other attributes (negating any likeness in attributes), and no partnership or sharing enters into His actions (negating any associates in His acts). This definition covers the essential aspects of tawḥīd as understood in Sunni theology: tawḥīd al-ulūhiyyah (worship), tawḥīd al-dhāt (essence), tawḥīd al-ṣifāt (attributes), and tawḥīd al-af‘āl (actions).

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever imagine Allah as having some similarity to something you've seen or known? The passage says His essence does not resemble any essence. Where in your thoughts do you need to purify your conception of Allah from any trace of resemblance? Make a firm intention today to learn the names and attributes as they are meant to be understood, without simile.
?
When you face a difficult situation, do you truly believe that no one else shares in Allah's actions—including your own efforts? The passage denies any partnership in His actions. In what area of your life are you acting as if your success depends on someone or something else besides Allah? Write down one area and consciously repeat: 'No partner in His actions' before you act.
?
You affirm that Allah's attributes are unique, but do you describe His attributes with an awareness that they are beyond your imagination? How does this affect your attitude in worship? Commit to prostrating tonight while consciously negating any mental image, focusing only on the meaning of His greatness.

Definition of Tawhid al-Shar'i and Its Three Meanings
Defines Tawhid al-Shar'i and lists three meanings of Tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the legal definition of Tawhid (al-Tawhid al-Shar'i) and be able to distinguish it from its linguistic and technical meanings.

Core Idea
Tawhid means singling out Allah in worship and affirming His oneness in essence, attributes, and actions.

This passage from al-Sanusi's commentary unpacks the key term 'Tawhid' used in the poem. It presents a precise definition of legal Tawhid and then clarifies two other meanings of the word: the linguistic meaning (simply 'oneness') and the technical meaning (the science of establishing religious beliefs). Understanding these distinctions is essential for grasping the theological framework of the course.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's main points.
Notice the three distinct definitions and how they are introduced.
2
Identify the definition of Tawhid al-Shar'i
Look for the sentence: 'إفراد المعبود بالعبادة، مع اعتقاد وحدته ذاتاً، وصفات، وأفعالا'. This is the key definition. Understand its components: singling out in worship and believing in His oneness in attributes.
This definition is the focus of the lesson and the poem.
3
Distinguish the three meanings of Tawhid
The passage lists three meanings: linguistic (knowledge of oneness), technical (the codified discipline of kalam), and legal (the definition itself). Compare how each differs from the others.
This helps prevent confusion between the science and the belief.
4
Reflect on why the legal definition matters
Consider why the author emphasizes that Tawhid is not just a science but a practical reality of worship. This connects to the overall purpose of kalam.
The legal definition is what the poem intends.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جَاءَ) أي: أرسله الله تعالى إلى جميع المُكلَّفين مِن الثَّقلين(۱) على رأس أربعينَ سَنةٍ من ولادته . (بالتَّوْحِيدِ) (۲) الشَّرعيّ ، وهو : إفراد المعبودِ بالعبادة، مع اعتقاد وَحْدَته ذاتاً ، وصفاتٍ ، وأفعالا ، فلا تقبل ذاته الانقسام بوجهٍ ، ولا تُشبه ذاتُه الذَّواتِ ، ولا تُشبه صفاتُه الصِّفاتِ ، ولا يدخل أفعاله الاشتراك . للتوحيد ثلاثة معان: أحدها لغوي، وهو العلم بأن الشيء واحدٌ . وثانيها : شر الفن المُّدوَّنِ ، وهو : (علم) يُقتدرُ به على إثبات العقائد الدينيَّة ، مكتسب من أدلتها اليقينيَّة). وثالثها: شرعي لا بمعنى الفن المدوّن ، وهو ما ذكره الشارح بقوله: (إفراد المعبودِ بالعبادة، اعتقاد وحدته، والتصديق بها ذاتا وصفات وأفعالا ـ الخ) .
(He came) i.e., Allah sent him to all obligated beings from the two weighty ones (al-thaqalayn) at the age of forty years from his birth. (With al-Tawhid) the legal al-Tawhid, which is: singling out the Worshipped One with worship, while believing in His oneness in essence, attributes, and actions. So His essence does not accept division in any way, nor does His essence resemble other essences, nor do His attributes resemble other attributes, and His actions do not admit partnership. Tawhid has three meanings: first, linguistic, which is knowledge that something is one. Second, the shar' of the codified discipline, which is: a science by which one is enabled to establish religious beliefs, acquired from their certain proofs). Third, legal (shar'i) not in the sense of the codified discipline, and it is what the commentator mentioned in his statement: 'Singling out the Worshipped One with worship, believing in His oneness, and affirming it in essence, attributes, and actions, etc.'
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage begins by explaining the phrase 'he came with al-Tawhid al-shar'i' from the poem. The commentator first expands on the meaning of 'came' as being sent by Allah to all obligated beings from humans and jinn at age 40. Then he defines al-Tawhid al-shar'i (legal Tawhid) as singling out the worshipped One with worship while believing in His oneness in essence, attributes, and actions. He then explains the three meanings of Tawhid: linguistic (knowledge of oneness), technical in the sense of the codified discipline of kalam (a science used to establish religious beliefs from certain proofs), and legal (shar'i) meaning which is the definition given by the commentator himself. This third meaning is the one intended in the line.

المكلفين
الثقلين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Confusing the technical meaning of Tawhid (the science) with the legal meaning (worshiping Allah alone).
Correction: The passage clearly distinguishes the second meaning (a science) from the third meaning (belief and worship). The legal Tawhid is not a body of knowledge but a practical reality.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The legal Tawhid is singling out Allah in worship with belief in His oneness, distinct from linguistic and technical meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After learning that legal Tawhid requires believing Allah's essence does not resemble anything, do you ever catch yourself imagining Allah in human-like forms? What practical step can you take today to purify your belief from such imaginations?
?
The lesson shows that Tawhid is not just a science to be studied but a call to worship alone. Examine your daily actions: do you ever perform acts seeking praise from others rather than Allah? How can you ensure your worship is solely for Him starting today?
?
You now know there are three meanings of Tawhid. Have you been satisfied with only knowing that Allah is one (linguistic) without acting on it by worshipping Him alone and affirming His attributes? What evidence from the passage compels you to live the legal Tawhid, and what is one change you will make?

The Definition of Religion in Kalam
Al-Sanusi's definition of religion with five components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of religion (ad-din) as a divine ordination with five essential components.

Core Idea
Religion is a divine ordination driving rational beings to eternal happiness through praiseworthy choice.

The study of Islamic theology begins with a clear understanding of religion itself. Al-Sanusi defines religion with precision, breaking it down into five necessary components that distinguish it from human systems. This passage presents that foundational definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic definition once and then the English translation. Note the five key terms.
The definition is concise; each word is deliberate.
2
Identify the five components
Break down the definition into: divine source, driving force, addressees, mode of choice, and ultimate goal.
These are: وضع إلهي, سائق, ذوي العقول, اختيار محمود, خير ذاتي.
3
Reflect on the necessity of each component
Consider why each part is needed to distinguish religion from human laws, coercion, or worldly benefits.
The commentary will expand on each.

أي : أحكام وضعها الله (٦) تعالى للعباد ، باعثةٌ إلى الخير الذاتي، وهو
(۱) قوله: (إلهي) أي : هذه الأحكام الموضوعة منسوبة للإله وهو الله ، وهو فصلٌ مُخرج للأوضاع البشرية، أي التي بكسبهم ، وذلك نحو القوانين السياسية التي ترجع إليها سياسة العالم، كعلم إصلاح المنزل، والأوضاع الصناعية كالنجارة فلا تسمى دينا، وكالكتب التي كان الحكماء يؤلفونها قديما في سياسة الرعية وإصلاح المدن فيحكم بها ملوك من لا شرع لهم ، فإنه وإن كان الخالق لكل الأشياء هو الله ، إلا أن البشر لهم في هذه كسب. فإن قلت: يلزم حينئذ أن أحكام الفقه الاجتهادية ليست من الدين لأن البشر لهم فيها كسب، وإنما منه ما ورد نصا لا خلاف فيه · أجيب: بأنها من الدين قطعا وهي موضوع إلهي غاية الأمر أنها تخفى علينا، والمجتهد يُعاني إظهارها والاستدلال عليها بقواعد الشرع، ولا مدخل له في وضعها. الشنواني. (۱) قوله: (وهي السعادة) يصح تذكير هذا الضمير وتأنيثه نظرا للمرجع والخير، وإنما كان الخير الذاتي هو السعادة؛ لأنها هي المقصودة بالذات والأصالة، وغيرها لا يبلغها في العظم، والسعادة الأبدية هي الجنة. الأمير. (۲) قوله: ( ويأتي آخر الموضوع) أي: في قوله: (وخص خير الخلق أن قد تمما به الجميع ربنا وعمما فافهم أن دينه عام، ودين غيره خاص، والمراد بآخر الموضوع: ما قابل الأول، والأولى أنْ يُراد بآخره قول المصنف : وحفظ دين ثم نفس مال نسب) فإن الشارح قال هناك: وهو ما شرعه الله لعباده من الأحكام عاما كشريعة نبينا محمدا ، أو خاصا كشريعة عیسی . الشنواني . (۳) قوله: (انقسامه) أي: الدين قوله : ( إلى عام ) كشريعته . وقوله : (وخاص) كشريعة عيسى ، وقيل : العام علم التوحيد والخاص علم فقه يخصها وأمور الدين أربعة كما قال النووي: أي علامات وجوده، ونظمها بعضهم فقال: أمور الدين صدق قصد وفا عهد وترك لمنهي كذا صحة العقد فصدق القصد أداء العبادة بالنية والإخلاص، ووفاء العهد: الإتيان بالفرائض ، وترك المنهي: اجتناب المحرمات ، وصحة العقد: جزمه بعقائد أهل السنة. الشنواني.
Explanation of the meaning of religion and its being devoid of tawhid before his mission. (3) A divine ordination (1), driving (2) those with intellects, by their praiseworthy choice (4), to what is good for them essentially (5). That is: rulings placed by God (6) Almighty for the servants, impelling to essential good, which is... (1) His saying: 'divine' means: these placed rulings are attributed to the divine, and He is God, and it is a differentia excluding human ordinations, i.e., those by their acquisition, such as political laws to which the governance of the world is referred, like the science of household management, and industrial ordinations like carpentry; these are not called religion, and like the books that the sages used to compose in ancient times on the governance of subjects and the reform of cities, by which kings of those who have no revealed law rule. For although the Creator of all things is God, humans have acquisition in these. If you say: then it would follow that ijtihādī rulings of fiqh are not part of religion because humans have acquisition in them, and only what is transmitted as a text with no dispute is from it. The answer: they are certainly from religion, and they are a divine subject; the only matter is that it is hidden from us, and the mujtahid undertakes to manifest them and deduce them by the rules of the Sharia, and he has no role in placing them. Al-Shanwānī. (2) His saying: 'driving' means: impelling and motivating; because the responsible person, when he hears what results from performing the obligatory deed of reward, is driven to do it, or hears what he obtains from performing the forbidden deed of punishment, is driven to leave it. It is a second differentia excluding divine ordinations that are not driving, like the growth of the earth and the raining of the sky. Thus some people have exemplified. Al-Shanwānī. (3) His saying: 'by their choice' excludes the coercive, like the pain that drives to moaning unwillingly. It contains that guidance is not necessary from this ordination, for this choice may be withheld from whom God wills to lead astray. Al-Amīr. (4) His saying: 'the praiseworthy' is in the accusative as an operative case of 'choice', meaning: He drives them due to their choice of the praiseworthy matter, which is Islam; and in the genitive it is an adjective for choice. By it the blameworthy is excluded, like the ability for disbelief; for it is a divine ordination driving those with intellects by their blameworthy choice, and like love of this world, for it is a divine ordination that drives those with intellects to abandon zakāt by their blameworthy choice. And whenever choice is praiseworthy, it only drives to good. So His saying: 'to what is good for them' He mentioned it as a means to His saying: 'essentially'. Al-Shanwānī. (5) His saying: 'essentially' and essentially good is eternal happiness; the divine ordinations driving for mere worldly benefit are excluded, like the faculties of crafts created in humans. Al-Amīr. (6) His saying: 'God placed it' know that the legal ruling, as previously mentioned, is applied to the complete relations that came = Explanation of the meaning of religion, and its being devoid of tawhid before his mission. (†) * Eternal happiness (1), and at the end of this topic (2) comes its division into general and special. = • So he guided the creation to the religion of truth with his sword and his guidance to the truth. (Fā) When the mentioned Prophet was sent, he guided the creation, i.e., all the two worlds, himself... from the Sharia, like the affirmation of obligation for prayer and zakāt, and it is applied to the attributes of taklīf which are: obligation, prohibition, dislike, recommendation, and permission. If this second meaning is intended, then difficulty arises in His saying: 'placed it', because placement is creation, so it indicates that they are originated, while the mentioned attributes are eternal, because obligation, for example, is an expression of God's eternal speech, indicating the request of action with determination. And it is answered: that the actual relation is originated when the responsible person exists, if it is taken into their concept, then they are originated. So His saying: 'God placed it' means in consideration of the actual relation, originated. And if the first meaning is intended, then no difficulty. The apparent meaning. And the meaning of 'God placed it' is: He renewed and established them after non-existence, and do not say: He brought them into existence; because this is what we mean by relations, and relations are mental considerations that have no existence. Al-Shanwānī. (1) His saying: 'and it is happiness' the tadhkīr and ta'nīth of this pronoun are permissible with regard to the referent and 'good'. And indeed essentially good is happiness because it is intended essentially and originally, and nothing else reaches it in greatness. And eternal happiness is Paradise. Al-Amīr. (2) His saying: 'and at the end of the topic' i.e., in his saying: 'And He specially honored the best of creation that He has indeed completed with him all, our Lord, and generalized, so understand that his religion is general, and the religion of others is particular.' The intended meaning of 'end of the topic' is what corresponds to the beginning. And it is better that what is intended by its end is the author's saying: 'And preserve religion, then life, then property, lineage.' For the commentator said there: And it is what God has legislated for His servants of rulings, general like the Sharia of our Prophet Muhammad, or particular like the Sharia of Jesus. Al-Shanwānī. (3) His saying: 'its division' i.e., of religion, his saying: 'into general' like his Sharia. And his saying: 'and special' like the Sharia of Jesus. And it is said: the general is the science of tawhid, and the special is the science of fiqh that pertains to it. The matters of religion are four, as al-Nawawī said: they are signs of its existence. Some organized them: The matters of religion: truthfulness of intention, fulfillment of covenant, leaving what is forbidden, and soundness of contract. Truthfulness of intention is performing worship with intention and sincerity. Fulfillment of covenant is performing the obligations. Leaving what is forbidden is avoiding the prohibitions. Soundness of contract is affirming with certainty the beliefs of Ahl al-Sunna. Al-Shanwānī.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part explanation of the meaning of religion (ad-din) and its relation to tawhid before the Prophet's mission. The author begins by defining religion as 'a divine ordination driving those with intellects, by their praiseworthy choice, to what is essentially good for them.' Each term in this definition is then expanded through footnotes. The first footnote clarifies that 'divine' (ilāhi) distinguishes religion from human-made laws, including political systems and crafts—though humans may acquire these, rulings of ijtihad are still divine in origin, only hidden. The second footnote explains 'driving' (sa'iq) as impelling through reward and punishment, excluding natural phenomena like rain. The third footnote specifies that choice is volitional, not coercive, and does not guarantee guidance. The fourth footnote distinguishes 'praiseworthy choice' as Islam, excluding blameworthy choices like disbelief. The fifth footnote identifies 'essentially good' as eternal happiness (sa'adat al-ākhira). The sixth footnote addresses a theological nuance: legal rulings as attributes (like obligation) are eternal, but their actual relation to created beings is originated—'place' (waḍ') refers to this originated relation. After this, the passage transitions to the Prophet's mission: 'So he guided the creation to the religion of truth with his sword and his guidance.' A commentary explains that the Prophet guided both humans and jinn, and addresses an objection about the word 'place' (waḍa'a) implying creation of rulings, which would conflict with the eternity of divine attributes. The answer distinguishes between the eternal attribute and the created relation. Finally, the passage discusses the division of religion into general (the Sharia of Muhammad) and specific (previous Sharias), and enumerates four pillars of religion: truthfulness of intention, fulfillment of covenant, leaving the forbidden, and soundness of belief.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That religion is a human invention or social contract.
Correction: The definition asserts religion's divine origin; even ijtihad rulings are based on divine sources.

Checkpoint
What is the ultimate goal (essential good) mentioned in the definition of religion?
A) Worldly success
B) Eternal happiness
C) Social order
D) Personal satisfaction

Key Takeaway
Religion is a divinely ordained system that rationally motivates humans to choose eternal bliss.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat your religion as a divine ordinance that drives you toward eternal happiness, or as a set of cultural habits? What would change if you took the 'driving' (sa'iq) aspect of your deen personally—every prayer, every fast, as a motivation from God toward your everlasting good?
?
The definition says religion addresses those with intellects (dhawi al-'uqul). Are you using your intellect to understand why you believe and practice, or are you blindly following? This week, choose one practice and reflect on why it leads to your essential good (khayr dhati) and eternal happiness.
?
Your religion is not simply your own choice; it is a divine ordination. Does this awe you? Does it make you more careful about how you approach your deen? Commit tonight to renew your intention (niyyah) in one act of worship, making it a conscious, praiseworthy choice.

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
5 min
Distinguishing Divine Law from Human Convention
Explanation of 'divine' and exclusion of human ordinations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the 'divine' component of religion excludes human laws and innovations, including the case of ijtihad rulings.

Core Idea
Religion is exclusively divine; human conventions and ijtihad rulings remain rooted in divine sources.

The first component of religion is its divine origin. This lesson examines what 'divine' means and what it excludes, such as political systems, crafts, and even the books of ancient sages. A key question about ijtihad rulings is addressed to show they are still part of divine law.

Guided Path
1
Read the footnote on 'divine'
Read the Arabic passage that explains 'ilāhi' and distinguishes divine from human ordinations.
Note the examples: political laws, carpentry, ancient governance books.
2
Understand the ijtihad objection and answer
The commentary raises an objection: if ijtihad rulings involve human effort, are they not human? Then it answers that the mujtahid only uncovers what is already divinely placed.
This preserves the divine nature of all Sharia rulings.
3
Summarize the exclusive claim
Religion is defined by its divine source; human systems, even if beneficial, are not religion.

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part explanation of the meaning of religion (ad-din) and its relation to tawhid before the Prophet's mission. The author begins by defining religion as 'a divine ordination driving those with intellects, by their praiseworthy choice, to what is essentially good for them.' Each term in this definition is then expanded through footnotes. The first footnote clarifies that 'divine' (ilāhi) distinguishes religion from human-made laws, including political systems and crafts—though humans may acquire these, rulings of ijtihad are still divine in origin, only hidden. The second footnote explains 'driving' (sa'iq) as impelling through reward and punishment, excluding natural phenomena like rain. The third footnote specifies that choice is volitional, not coercive, and does not guarantee guidance. The fourth footnote distinguishes 'praiseworthy choice' as Islam, excluding blameworthy choices like disbelief. The fifth footnote identifies 'essentially good' as eternal happiness (sa'adat al-ākhira). The sixth footnote addresses a theological nuance: legal rulings as attributes (like obligation) are eternal, but their actual relation to created beings is originated—'place' (waḍ') refers to this originated relation. After this, the passage transitions to the Prophet's mission: 'So he guided the creation to the religion of truth with his sword and his guidance.' A commentary explains that the Prophet guided both humans and jinn, and addresses an objection about the word 'place' (waḍa'a) implying creation of rulings, which would conflict with the eternity of divine attributes. The answer distinguishes between the eternal attribute and the created relation. Finally, the passage discusses the division of religion into general (the Sharia of Muhammad) and specific (previous Sharias), and enumerates four pillars of religion: truthfulness of intention, fulfillment of covenant, leaving the forbidden, and soundness of belief.

Key Terms
وضع إلهي
أوضاع بشرية
قوانين سياسية
أحكام اجتهادية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That ijtihad rulings are human inventions and not part of divine religion.
Correction: The passage affirms they are divine; the mujtahid only uncovers them.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are ijtihad rulings still considered divine?
A) Because they are derived from human reason alone
B) Because they are based on political systems
C) Because the mujtahid only uncovers what is already placed by God
D) Because they are not part of religion

Key Takeaway
The divine origin of religion excludes all human-made systems, while preserving the divine nature of ijtihad as discovery.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you follow a religious ruling from a scholar, do you think of it as that scholar's creation or as their discovery of God's law? How does your answer affect your trust and submission to that ruling?
?
Are there areas in your life where you treat human-made systems—like cultural norms or personal preferences—as if they were divine commands? What would it mean to truly submit only to what is divine?
?
If all your knowledge of religion came through human intermediaries, how do you ensure that you are not conflating their interpretation with divine revelation itself? What steps can you take to anchor your heart in the source texts?

The Driving Force and Voluntary Choice
Explanation of 'driving' and 'by their choice' with exclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how religion drives through reward and punishment and how choice must be voluntary, not coercive.

Core Idea
Religion motivates through promises and threats, but acceptance requires voluntary praiseworthy choice.

The definition includes 'driving' and 'by their choice.' This lesson explores the mechanism of religious motivation and the crucial distinction between coerced and voluntary acceptance. The commentary clarifies that guidance is not guaranteed even if the ordination is present.

Guided Path
1
Read the footnotes on 'driving' and 'choice'
Read the Arabic passages for footnotes 2 and 3. Notice how 'driving' is explained through reward and punishment.
Also see the exclusion of natural phenomena and coercion.
2
Understand the effect of choice
Choice is voluntary; it excludes compulsion like pain causing moaning. Guidance depends on divine will, not just the presence of the ordination.
The passage emphasizes human agency but within divine decree.
3
Contrast with blameworthy choice
The next lesson will detail 'praiseworthy choice'; here we see that blameworthy choice leads to evil.
This connects to the concept of ikhtiyar mahmud vs madhmum.

Explanation of the meaning of religion and its being devoid of tawhid before his mission. (3) A divine ordination (1), driving (2) those with intellects, by their praiseworthy choice (4), to what is good for them essentially (5). That is: rulings placed by God (6) Almighty for the servants, impelling to essential good, which is... (1) His saying: 'divine' means: these placed rulings are attributed to the divine, and He is God, and it is a differentia excluding human ordinations, i.e., those by their acquisition, such as political laws to which the governance of the world is referred, like the science of household management, and industrial ordinations like carpentry; these are not called religion, and like the books that the sages used to compose in ancient times on the governance of subjects and the reform of cities, by which kings of those who have no revealed law rule. For although the Creator of all things is God, humans have acquisition in these. If you say: then it would follow that ijtihādī rulings of fiqh are not part of religion because humans have acquisition in them, and only what is transmitted as a text with no dispute is from it. The answer: they are certainly from religion, and they are a divine subject; the only matter is that it is hidden from us, and the mujtahid undertakes to manifest them and deduce them by the rules of the Sharia, and he has no role in placing them. Al-Shanwānī. (2) His saying: 'driving' means: impelling and motivating; because the responsible person, when he hears what results from performing the obligatory deed of reward, is driven to do it, or hears what he obtains from performing the forbidden deed of punishment, is driven to leave it. It is a second differentia excluding divine ordinations that are not driving, like the growth of the earth and the raining of the sky. Thus some people have exemplified. Al-Shanwānī. (3) His saying: 'by their choice' excludes the coercive, like the pain that drives to moaning unwillingly. It contains that guidance is not necessary from this ordination, for this choice may be withheld from whom God wills to lead astray. Al-Amīr. (4) His saying: 'the praiseworthy' is in the accusative as an operative case of 'choice', meaning: He drives them due to their choice of the praiseworthy matter, which is Islam; and in the genitive it is an adjective for choice. By it the blameworthy is excluded, like the ability for disbelief; for it is a divine ordination driving those with intellects by their blameworthy choice, and like love of this world, for it is a divine ordination that drives those with intellects to abandon zakāt by their blameworthy choice. And whenever choice is praiseworthy, it only drives to good. So His saying: 'to what is good for them' He mentioned it as a means to His saying: 'essentially'. Al-Shanwānī. (5) His saying: 'essentially' and essentially good is eternal happiness; the divine ordinations driving for mere worldly benefit are excluded, like the faculties of crafts created in humans. Al-Amīr. (6) His saying: 'God placed it' know that the legal ruling, as previously mentioned, is applied to the complete relations that came = Explanation of the meaning of religion, and its being devoid of tawhid before his mission. (†) * Eternal happiness (1), and at the end of this topic (2) comes its division into general and special. = • So he guided the creation to the religion of truth with his sword and his guidance to the truth. (Fā) When the mentioned Prophet was sent, he guided the creation, i.e., all the two worlds, himself... from the Sharia, like the affirmation of obligation for prayer and zakāt, and it is applied to the attributes of taklīf which are: obligation, prohibition, dislike, recommendation, and permission. If this second meaning is intended, then difficulty arises in His saying: 'placed it', because placement is creation, so it indicates that they are originated, while the mentioned attributes are eternal, because obligation, for example, is an expression of God's eternal speech, indicating the request of action with determination. And it is answered: that the actual relation is originated when the responsible person exists, if it is taken into their concept, then they are originated. So His saying: 'God placed it' means in consideration of the actual relation, originated. And if the first meaning is intended, then no difficulty. The apparent meaning. And the meaning of 'God placed it' is: He renewed and established them after non-existence, and do not say: He brought them into existence; because this is what we mean by relations, and relations are mental considerations that have no existence. Al-Shanwānī. (1) His saying: 'and it is happiness' the tadhkīr and ta'nīth of this pronoun are permissible with regard to the referent and 'good'. And indeed essentially good is happiness because it is intended essentially and originally, and nothing else reaches it in greatness. And eternal happiness is Paradise. Al-Amīr. (2) His saying: 'and at the end of the topic' i.e., in his saying: 'And He specially honored the best of creation that He has indeed completed with him all, our Lord, and generalized, so understand that his religion is general, and the religion of others is particular.' The intended meaning of 'end of the topic' is what corresponds to the beginning. And it is better that what is intended by its end is the author's saying: 'And preserve religion, then life, then property, lineage.' For the commentator said there: And it is what God has legislated for His servants of rulings, general like the Sharia of our Prophet Muhammad, or particular like the Sharia of Jesus. Al-Shanwānī. (3) His saying: 'its division' i.e., of religion, his saying: 'into general' like his Sharia. And his saying: 'and special' like the Sharia of Jesus. And it is said: the general is the science of tawhid, and the special is the science of fiqh that pertains to it. The matters of religion are four, as al-Nawawī said: they are signs of its existence. Some organized them: The matters of religion: truthfulness of intention, fulfillment of covenant, leaving what is forbidden, and soundness of contract. Truthfulness of intention is performing worship with intention and sincerity. Fulfillment of covenant is performing the obligations. Leaving what is forbidden is avoiding the prohibitions. Soundness of contract is affirming with certainty the beliefs of Ahl al-Sunna. Al-Shanwānī.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part explanation of the meaning of religion (ad-din) and its relation to tawhid before the Prophet's mission. The author begins by defining religion as 'a divine ordination driving those with intellects, by their praiseworthy choice, to what is essentially good for them.' Each term in this definition is then expanded through footnotes. The first footnote clarifies that 'divine' (ilāhi) distinguishes religion from human-made laws, including political systems and crafts—though humans may acquire these, rulings of ijtihad are still divine in origin, only hidden. The second footnote explains 'driving' (sa'iq) as impelling through reward and punishment, excluding natural phenomena like rain. The third footnote specifies that choice is volitional, not coercive, and does not guarantee guidance. The fourth footnote distinguishes 'praiseworthy choice' as Islam, excluding blameworthy choices like disbelief. The fifth footnote identifies 'essentially good' as eternal happiness (sa'adat al-ākhira). The sixth footnote addresses a theological nuance: legal rulings as attributes (like obligation) are eternal, but their actual relation to created beings is originated—'place' (waḍ') refers to this originated relation. After this, the passage transitions to the Prophet's mission: 'So he guided the creation to the religion of truth with his sword and his guidance.' A commentary explains that the Prophet guided both humans and jinn, and addresses an objection about the word 'place' (waḍa'a) implying creation of rulings, which would conflict with the eternity of divine attributes. The answer distinguishes between the eternal attribute and the created relation. Finally, the passage discusses the division of religion into general (the Sharia of Muhammad) and specific (previous Sharias), and enumerates four pillars of religion: truthfulness of intention, fulfillment of covenant, leaving the forbidden, and soundness of belief.

اختيار
ثواب
عقاب
قهري
إضلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That religion forces belief or that natural events are part of religious law.
Correction: Religion motivates through informed choice, not compulsion, and natural events are not religious ordinances.

Checkpoint
What do the examples 'growth of the earth' and 'raining of the sky' illustrate?
A) They are examples of religious duties
B) They are divine ordinations that are not driving
C) They are coercive forces
D) They are result of human choice

Key Takeaway
Religion motivates through promises and threats, but personal acceptance remains voluntary and subject to divine will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that Allah alone grants the praiseworthy choice to accept faith, what does that mean for your own daily efforts to strengthen your īmān? Are you relying on your own efforts or begging Allah for that gift?
?
The lesson teaches that religion drives through promise and threat, yet you may still feel lazy in worship. When you hear the threat of punishment, does it move you to action, or have you become desensitized? What will you do this week to let that driving force actually change your behavior?

What Is Kalam?

Praiseworthy Choice and Essential Good
Explanation of 'praiseworthy choice' and 'essential good' with exclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of 'praiseworthy choice' (Islam) and 'essential good' (eternal happiness), and how they exclude blameworthy choices and worldly benefits.

Core Idea
Praiseworthy choice is Islam, leading to eternal happiness, not to worldly or blameworthy ends.

The definition specifies 'praiseworthy choice' and 'essential good.' This lesson clarifies that not all choices are equal—only the choice of Islam is praiseworthy, and the ultimate good is the afterlife, not worldly gain. The commentary provides stark examples of blameworthy choices like disbelief and love of this world.

Guided Path
1
Read footnotes 4 and 5
Read the Arabic passages explaining 'praiseworthy choice' and 'essential good.'
Notice how 'praiseworthy' is grammatically linked to choice.
2
Understand the exclusion of blameworthy choice
Examples: ability for disbelief, love of this world—these are divine ordinations but drive to evil due to blameworthy choice.
The passage emphasizes that praiseworthy choice only leads to good.
3
Understand essential good as eternal happiness
Essential good (khayr dhati) is distinguished from worldly benefits; it is the ultimate goal of religion.
Worldly crafts are excluded as they are not essential goods.

(فَ) لمَّا بُعثَ النَّبِيُّ المذكورُ أَرْشَدَ الخلْقَ) أي : جميعَ التَّقلينِ ، بنفسه عن الشرع، كثبوت الوجوب للصلاة والزكاة، ويُطلق علي أوصاف التكليف التي هي: الإيجاب، والتحريم والكراهة، والندب والإباحة، فإن أريد هذا الثاني .. أشكلَ قوله : (وضعها) لأن الوضع عبارة عن الخلق ، فيفيد أنها حادثة ، مع أن الأوصاف المذكورة قديمة ، لأن الإيجاب مثلا: عبارة عن خطاب الله القديم، عن خطاب الله القديم، الدال على طلب الفعل طلبا جازماً. ويُجاب: بأن التعلق التنجيزي الحادث عند وجود المكلف إن أخذ في مفهومها فهي حادثة ، فقوله: (وضعها الله) أي باعتبار التعلق التنجيزي ،الحادث، وإن أردت الأول فلا إشكال، الظاهر. ومعنى وضعها الله : أي: جددها وأثبتها بعد عدم ، ولا تقل: أوجدها ؛ لأن وهذا هو مرادنا بها النسب والنسب أمور اعتبارية لا وجود لها . الشنواني . (۱) قوله: (وهي السعادة) يصح تذكير هذا الضمير وتأنيثه نظرا للمرجع والخير، وإنما كان الخير الذاتي هو السعادة؛ لأنها هي المقصودة بالذات والأصالة، وغيرها لا يبلغها في العظم، والسعادة الأبدية هي الجنة. الأمير. (۲) قوله: ( ويأتي آخر الموضوع) أي: في قوله: (وخص خير الخلق أن قد تمما به الجميع ربنا وعمما فافهم أن دينه عام، ودين غيره خاص، والمراد بآخر الموضوع: ما قابل الأول، والأولى أنْ يُراد بآخره قول المصنف : وحفظ دين ثم نفس مال نسب) فإن الشارح قال هناك: وهو ما شرعه الله لعباده من الأحكام عاما كشريعة نبينا محمدا ، أو خاصا كشريعة عیسی . الشنواني . (۳) قوله: (انقسامه) أي: الدين قوله : ( إلى عام ) كشريعته . وقوله : (وخاص) كشريعة عيسى ، وقيل : العام علم التوحيد والخاص علم فقه يخصها وأمور الدين أربعة كما قال النووي: أي علامات وجوده، ونظمها بعضهم فقال: أمور الدين صدق قصد وفا عهد وترك لمنهي كذا صحة العقد فصدق القصد أداء العبادة بالنية والإخلاص، ووفاء العهد: الإتيان بالفرائض ، وترك المنهي: اجتناب المحرمات ، وصحة العقد: جزمه بعقائد أهل السنة. الشنواني.
Explanation of the meaning of religion and its being devoid of tawhid before his mission. (3) A divine ordination (1), driving (2) those with intellects, by their praiseworthy choice (4), to what is good for them essentially (5). That is: rulings placed by God (6) Almighty for the servants, impelling to essential good, which is... (1) His saying: 'divine' means: these placed rulings are attributed to the divine, and He is God, and it is a differentia excluding human ordinations, i.e., those by their acquisition, such as political laws to which the governance of the world is referred, like the science of household management, and industrial ordinations like carpentry; these are not called religion, and like the books that the sages used to compose in ancient times on the governance of subjects and the reform of cities, by which kings of those who have no revealed law rule. For although the Creator of all things is God, humans have acquisition in these. If you say: then it would follow that ijtihādī rulings of fiqh are not part of religion because humans have acquisition in them, and only what is transmitted as a text with no dispute is from it. The answer: they are certainly from religion, and they are a divine subject; the only matter is that it is hidden from us, and the mujtahid undertakes to manifest them and deduce them by the rules of the Sharia, and he has no role in placing them. Al-Shanwānī. (2) His saying: 'driving' means: impelling and motivating; because the responsible person, when he hears what results from performing the obligatory deed of reward, is driven to do it, or hears what he obtains from performing the forbidden deed of punishment, is driven to leave it. It is a second differentia excluding divine ordinations that are not driving, like the growth of the earth and the raining of the sky. Thus some people have exemplified. Al-Shanwānī. (3) His saying: 'by their choice' excludes the coercive, like the pain that drives to moaning unwillingly. It contains that guidance is not necessary from this ordination, for this choice may be withheld from whom God wills to lead astray. Al-Amīr. (4) His saying: 'the praiseworthy' is in the accusative as an operative case of 'choice', meaning: He drives them due to their choice of the praiseworthy matter, which is Islam; and in the genitive it is an adjective for choice. By it the blameworthy is excluded, like the ability for disbelief; for it is a divine ordination driving those with intellects by their blameworthy choice, and like love of this world, for it is a divine ordination that drives those with intellects to abandon zakāt by their blameworthy choice. And whenever choice is praiseworthy, it only drives to good. So His saying: 'to what is good for them' He mentioned it as a means to His saying: 'essentially'. Al-Shanwānī. (5) His saying: 'essentially' and essentially good is eternal happiness; the divine ordinations driving for mere worldly benefit are excluded, like the faculties of crafts created in humans. Al-Amīr. (6) His saying: 'God placed it' know that the legal ruling, as previously mentioned, is applied to the complete relations that came = Explanation of the meaning of religion, and its being devoid of tawhid before his mission. (†) * Eternal happiness (1), and at the end of this topic (2) comes its division into general and special. = • So he guided the creation to the religion of truth with his sword and his guidance to the truth. (Fā) When the mentioned Prophet was sent, he guided the creation, i.e., all the two worlds, himself... from the Sharia, like the affirmation of obligation for prayer and zakāt, and it is applied to the attributes of taklīf which are: obligation, prohibition, dislike, recommendation, and permission. If this second meaning is intended, then difficulty arises in His saying: 'placed it', because placement is creation, so it indicates that they are originated, while the mentioned attributes are eternal, because obligation, for example, is an expression of God's eternal speech, indicating the request of action with determination. And it is answered: that the actual relation is originated when the responsible person exists, if it is taken into their concept, then they are originated. So His saying: 'God placed it' means in consideration of the actual relation, originated. And if the first meaning is intended, then no difficulty. The apparent meaning. And the meaning of 'God placed it' is: He renewed and established them after non-existence, and do not say: He brought them into existence; because this is what we mean by relations, and relations are mental considerations that have no existence. Al-Shanwānī. (1) His saying: 'and it is happiness' the tadhkīr and ta'nīth of this pronoun are permissible with regard to the referent and 'good'. And indeed essentially good is happiness because it is intended essentially and originally, and nothing else reaches it in greatness. And eternal happiness is Paradise. Al-Amīr. (2) His saying: 'and at the end of the topic' i.e., in his saying: 'And He specially honored the best of creation that He has indeed completed with him all, our Lord, and generalized, so understand that his religion is general, and the religion of others is particular.' The intended meaning of 'end of the topic' is what corresponds to the beginning. And it is better that what is intended by its end is the author's saying: 'And preserve religion, then life, then property, lineage.' For the commentator said there: And it is what God has legislated for His servants of rulings, general like the Sharia of our Prophet Muhammad, or particular like the Sharia of Jesus. Al-Shanwānī. (3) His saying: 'its division' i.e., of religion, his saying: 'into general' like his Sharia. And his saying: 'and special' like the Sharia of Jesus. And it is said: the general is the science of tawhid, and the special is the science of fiqh that pertains to it. The matters of religion are four, as al-Nawawī said: they are signs of its existence. Some organized them: The matters of religion: truthfulness of intention, fulfillment of covenant, leaving what is forbidden, and soundness of contract. Truthfulness of intention is performing worship with intention and sincerity. Fulfillment of covenant is performing the obligations. Leaving what is forbidden is avoiding the prohibitions. Soundness of contract is affirming with certainty the beliefs of Ahl al-Sunna. Al-Shanwānī.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part explanation of the meaning of religion (ad-din) and its relation to tawhid before the Prophet's mission. The author begins by defining religion as 'a divine ordination driving those with intellects, by their praiseworthy choice, to what is essentially good for them.' Each term in this definition is then expanded through footnotes. The first footnote clarifies that 'divine' (ilāhi) distinguishes religion from human-made laws, including political systems and crafts—though humans may acquire these, rulings of ijtihad are still divine in origin, only hidden. The second footnote explains 'driving' (sa'iq) as impelling through reward and punishment, excluding natural phenomena like rain. The third footnote specifies that choice is volitional, not coercive, and does not guarantee guidance. The fourth footnote distinguishes 'praiseworthy choice' as Islam, excluding blameworthy choices like disbelief. The fifth footnote identifies 'essentially good' as eternal happiness (sa'adat al-ākhira). The sixth footnote addresses a theological nuance: legal rulings as attributes (like obligation) are eternal, but their actual relation to created beings is originated—'place' (waḍ') refers to this originated relation. After this, the passage transitions to the Prophet's mission: 'So he guided the creation to the religion of truth with his sword and his guidance.' A commentary explains that the Prophet guided both humans and jinn, and addresses an objection about the word 'place' (waḍa'a) implying creation of rulings, which would conflict with the eternity of divine attributes. The answer distinguishes between the eternal attribute and the created relation. Finally, the passage discusses the division of religion into general (the Sharia of Muhammad) and specific (previous Sharias), and enumerates four pillars of religion: truthfulness of intention, fulfillment of covenant, leaving the forbidden, and soundness of belief.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any divine ordination that leads to worldly benefit is part of religion.
Correction: Only ordinations that lead to eternal happiness through praiseworthy choice are religion.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of blameworthy choice according to the passage?
A) Performing zakah
B) Having the ability to disbelieve
C) Choosing Islam
D) Acquiring a craft

Key Takeaway
Religion's purpose is to guide rational beings through commendable choice to eternal happiness, not worldly gain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson defines praiseworthy choice as Islam itself, and it leads only to good. When you look at your daily decisions, how many are truly motivated by your commitment to Islam versus by love of this world? What is one practical change you can make today to ensure your choices align with the praiseworthy choice that leads to eternal happiness?
?
The lesson teaches that essential good is eternal happiness (Paradise), not worldly benefits. Have you ever secretly wished that following Islam would bring you more worldly success or comfort? How might this hidden expectation be a form of blameworthy choice? Repent from this today and reaffirm that your hope is in the Hereafter.

What Is Kalam?
advanced
6 min
Theological Nuance: Are Divine Rulings Created?
Theological resolution of 'placed' implying created rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'placed' (waḍa'a) in the definition of religion refers to the originated relation of rulings, not the eternal attribute of God.

Core Idea
Divine rulings are eternal as attributes but created as relations to human actions.

The definition includes 'placed' (waḍa‘), which might imply creation. But God's attributes are eternal. This lesson resolves the apparent conflict by distinguishing between the eternal attribute of commanding and the temporal relation that arises when a responsible person exists. The commentary shows how the term 'placed' is correctly understood.

Guided Path
1
Read footnote 6 carefully
This is a dense theological discussion. Read the Arabic passage that addresses the objection.
The objection is that 'placed' implies creation, conflicting with the eternity of divine attributes.
2
Understand the distinction
The answer distinguishes between the eternal attribute (e.g., God's command) and the 'actual relation' (al-ta‘alluq al-tanjīzī) that comes into existence when the person is created.
The term 'placed' refers to this temporal relation.
3
Note the alternative reading
If we take 'rulings' as the complete relations (al-nisab al-tāmmah), they are mental considerations with no external existence.
This avoids the problem of creation.

أي : أحكام وضعها الله (٦) تعالى للعباد ، باعثةٌ إلى الخير الذاتي، وهو

The intended meaning of 'end of the topic' is what corresponds to the beginning. And it is better that what is intended by its end is the author's saying: 'And preserve religion, then life, then property, lineage.' For the commentator said there: And it is what God has legislated for His servants of rulings, general like the Sharia of our Prophet Muhammad, or particular like the Sharia of Jesus. Al-Shanwānī. (3) His saying: 'its division' i.e., of religion, his saying: 'into general' like his Sharia. And his saying: 'and special' like the Sharia of Jesus. And it is said: the general is the science of tawhid, and the special is the science of fiqh that pertains to it. The matters of religion are four, as al-Nawawī said: they are signs of its existence. Some organized them: The matters of religion: truthfulness of intention, fulfillment of covenant, leaving what is forbidden, and soundness of contract. Truthfulness of intention is performing worship with intention and sincerity. Fulfillment of covenant is performing the obligations. Leaving what is forbidden is avoiding the prohibitions. Soundness of contract is affirming with certainty the beliefs of Ahl al-Sunna. Al-Shanwānī.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part explanation of the meaning of religion (ad-din) and its relation to tawhid before the Prophet's mission. The author begins by defining religion as 'a divine ordination driving those with intellects, by their praiseworthy choice, to what is essentially good for them.' Each term in this definition is then expanded through footnotes. The first footnote clarifies that 'divine' (ilāhi) distinguishes religion from human-made laws, including political systems and crafts—though humans may acquire these, rulings of ijtihad are still divine in origin, only hidden. The second footnote explains 'driving' (sa'iq) as impelling through reward and punishment, excluding natural phenomena like rain. The third footnote specifies that choice is volitional, not coercive, and does not guarantee guidance. The fourth footnote distinguishes 'praiseworthy choice' as Islam, excluding blameworthy choices like disbelief. The fifth footnote identifies 'essentially good' as eternal happiness (sa'adat al-ākhira). The sixth footnote addresses a theological nuance: legal rulings as attributes (like obligation) are eternal, but their actual relation to created beings is originated—'place' (waḍ') refers to this originated relation. After this, the passage transitions to the Prophet's mission: 'So he guided the creation to the religion of truth with his sword and his guidance.' A commentary explains that the Prophet guided both humans and jinn, and addresses an objection about the word 'place' (waḍa'a) implying creation of rulings, which would conflict with the eternity of divine attributes. The answer distinguishes between the eternal attribute and the created relation. Finally, the passage discusses the division of religion into general (the Sharia of Muhammad) and specific (previous Sharias), and enumerates four pillars of religion: truthfulness of intention, fulfillment of covenant, leaving the forbidden, and soundness of belief.

وضع
حكم شرعي
نسب تامة
تعلق تنجيزي
قديم
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the term 'placed' in the definition contradicts the eternity of God's attributes.
Correction: The passage explains that 'placed' refers to the temporal relation, not the eternal attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is the term 'placed' (وضع) understood when applied to the attributes of taklif?
A) It means created from nothing
B) It refers to the eternal attribute itself
C) It refers to the actual relation that occurs when the responsible person exists
D) It means the rulings are not real

Key Takeaway
The term 'placed' in the definition of religion is a theological reference to the temporal relation of rulings, preserving the eternity of God's attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear 'prayer is obligatory,' do you treat it as a timeless command from God or as a temporary rule for this world? How does your answer affect your consistency in prayer?
?
The passage distinguishes between God's eternal attribute of commanding and the temporal relation when you exist. Does this distinction make God's commands more real and urgent to you, or less? Why?
?
If the actual obligation only arises when you exist, does that give you an excuse to delay obedience? How does this lesson challenge your tendency to postpone what God has commanded?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate

The Prophet's Mission and the Division of Religion
Prophet's role in guidance and division of religion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of the Prophet in guiding creation and the division of religion into general (Sharia of Muhammad) and special (previous Sharias).

Core Idea
The Prophet guided all creation to the truth; his Sharia is general, while previous Sharias were specific to their communities.

After defining religion, the passage turns to the Prophet's mission. It describes how he guided both humans and jinn to the religion of truth through sword and guidance. A later commentary explains the division of religion into general and special, and lists four pillars that constitute the essence of religious practice.

Guided Path
1
Read the Prophet's guidance passage
Read the Arabic that begins 'فَأَرْشَدَ الخَلْقَ لِدِينِ الحَقِّ' and its commentary.
Note the mention of 'sword' and 'guidance'.
2
Understand the division into general and special
The passage states that the religion of Muhammad is general (shari‘ah ‘ammah), while that of previous prophets is special (shari‘ah khassah).
Some say general is tawhid, special is fiqh.
3
Learn the four pillars of religion
The commentary lists: truthfulness of intention, fulfillment of covenant, leaving the forbidden, and soundness of belief.
These summarize the essence of religion.
